SEND FOR NEW ILLUSTRATED CATALOGUE.
The Co-Operative
SYSTEM, the nearest approach to Socialism that can be achieved under the
present conditions of life, has met with phenomenal success wherever it has
been applied to any object. In England, even the Co-operative Societies
benefit some millions of persons—their membership exceeding a million and
a-half, and hold invested capital to the amount of eleven millions of money.
This success is owing to the fact that the investor secs not only a fair cofn-
■ mercial return on his investment, but a proprietary division of profits, which
he would not receive from any other organisation. It has been left to Aus-
tralia to apply this communistic principle to
Irrigation
and the CO-OPERATIVE IRRIGATION and MERCANTILE SOCIETY
of .AUSTRALIA, Ltd., have already secured three irrigation colonies in
Victoria, which are partially settled and planted, and are negotiating for the
purchase of a tract of magnificent irrigation country in the West. Nego-
tiations are also pending for a Government concession of Murray land, and
there are now
Colonies
half-populated, awaiting the small Capitalist, the employed ARTISAN, or
the man whose sole CAPITAL is his LABOUR.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 5, 1891.
The Abolition of Lawyers.
About two-fifths, or possibly one-half, of the
civilised human race is made up of commer-
cial and other cormorants of various degrees.
The old, baseless copy-book assertion, accord-
J ng to which industry is the sure road to
affluence, is an extinct superstition, and has
died an unnatural death of hunger and old
age. The way to affluence is a road that is
paved with commissions, and pickings, and
perquisites, and miscellaneous steals. The
self-made individual still serves as a bright
and shining light to the rising generation,
but the assertion that he rose through
industry and profound integrity is a lie and
an exploded tradition. Nobody, under any
cii’cunxstances whatsoever, does rise through
.sheer industry. By dixit of toil and mean-
ness it is possible to rise high enough to
become an employer and embezzle the fruits
of other men's labour, or to become a mom -
polist, and clean out other xxien by specula-
tive enterprises, but the person who rises by
honest energy only rises to be a cockatoo
farmer, or a boss stonebreakcr, or the prin-
cipal pick-axxd-shovel nxaxx in a road-gang.
Beyond these grades there is xxothing but
the great army of cormorants the countless
hosts of merchants, brokers, jobbers, bag-
men, dealers, accountants, bankers, agents,
lawyers, and others of the same kind, who
xxxake up that complex system which is called
civilisatioix. And the torpid x'ace of those
who are eaten have learned to accept (his
state of things as one of the xxecessities of
life, and to look upon the eaters as essential
members of the great scheme of Providence.
Such a proposition as the utter abolition of
lawyers iix all their varieties would probably
be looked upon as three-parts unreasoning
Vandalism and one-part coxxfirmed insanity,
for though the lawyer is admittedly a
bird of prey he is a constitutional and
old-established bird of prey —an elo-
quent bird of prey—the sort of bird
of prey who can only grow in an edu-
cated and refined community, and he is one
of the landmarks of progress, and a deep-
rooted institution generally. Therefore,
when- The Bulletin suggests that the
lawyer—as one among the great assemblage
of evil parasites—should be abolished so
utterly that ixo one shall ever find him
again, and that people will even forget that
he ever existed, it fully expects to be
denounced as a Vandal, and rather likes the
term than otherwise. Even the \ andal had
sundry good points, which history has
mostly ignored.
It is freely admitted by three-fourths of
the nation that the lawyer is a necessary
evil ; The Bulletin, on the other hand,
admits the evil and absolutely denies the
necessity. Or, if ho is necessary, it is
merely because he makes his own necessity.
He is an animal that travels in a circle, and
he is essential on account of his existence ;
if he did not exist he would not he essen-
tial. In other words, he lives to fill a
certain purpose, and he first of ail makes
the purpose, and then fills it. He is the
sole cause of himself, and he is also the effect
of his own cause. The business of his life is
to dig a hole, and then fill it up again, and
to charge both for the digging and the
filling; he also creates the original necessity
for the hole, and charges for that likewise.
Therefore if he is necessary he is necessary
only to himself, and if he supplies a long-
felt want it is his own want, and is only felt
by himself. And by-and-hye the nation will
realise that the man who wants himself, but
who is wanted by nobody else, has no right
to charge the public for supplying himself,
any more than the man who is hungry
should.charge for being hungry, and then
charge for eating, and finally send in a third
ponderous bill for not feeling hungry after
he has finished eating.
* * *
The lawyer exists, in the first place, to
expound the law. There is absolutely no
reason why (he law should want to be ex-
pounded at great expense, any more than
the newspaper ; if the law were made to be
as simple and as easy of comprehension as a
decently-written newspaper, then the race of
lawyers would presently become extinct,
and if the newspapers were made as dark
and profound and complicated as the law,
then a new race of practitioners would arise,
who would charge many guineas for reveal-
ing the hidden meaning of an item about a
drunk being fined ss. The cause of this
state of things is in every sense the lawyer
himself. In the first place, he makes the
law. The Legislature of every “civilised”
country is full of barristers and solicitors,
and all important measures are drafted by
one or other of those persons. They are
drawn up, not in the language of the country,
but in a strange and murky gibberish, for
the sufficient reason that the legal prac-
titioner is mentally fuddled by the influence
of many huge blue documents filled with
interminable miles of a decomposed dialect
of his own. Therefore, he is necessary, in
the first place, to construct a statute, fox no
one save himself can write that strange and
battered Yolapuk in which tradition demands
all statutes should be written ; and he is
necessary again, in the second place, because
no one else can comprehend it when it is
written And, in addition to all this, lie is
required for all manner of archseological
researches, for the country is full of old, for-
gotten statutes, which no one has time to
hunt up excepting a man who gives his whole
life to the task ; and yet, unless they are
hunted up, and a watchful eye kept upon
them, no one can tell whether he may not
bo liable to death by judicial strangulation
for wheeling a bai’row in the street con-
trary to the regulations of some dead
and dusty monarch of old, whose bones
have long since degenerated into grit, and
whose memory is forgotten, and his monu-
ment sold for a gatepost. These weird old
statutes originally existed because they wore
made by lawyers ; they keep on existing
because the great army of lawyers who
crowd the Legislature have so far successfully
resisted every effort towards their abolition ;
and the lawyer himself exists because of the
existence of the laws. Thus he goes round
in a never-ending circle, and his head is
caused by his tail, and his tail is the origin
and reason of his head ; and he is present
because he is a public necessity, and he is a
public necessity because he is present, and
for no other motive whatever.
# # #
The lawyer, however, makes only a small
part of his profits by expounding the misty
and muddled code of the land. The largest
part of his takings are gained by picking
holes in the statutes, and perverting justice
by reason of some shadow of doubt which
rests upon some obscure clause or section or
sub section of the Act. For, because of the
existence rf the lawyer, the simplest Act of
Parliament must not only state conclusively
what it does mean, bub must also state, with
a long weariness of incessant iteration that
it does not mean anything else in heaven or
earth or sea, or any other place or state or
condition whatsoever. And as no Act ever
can enumerate a I the things it does not
mean, and state in tones of thunder that it
does not mean any of them, therefore there
are from one to a dozen flaws in every
statute ; and in every case which is heard in
the courts there arc from one to a dozen
costly advocates hired to find technical ob-
jections, and a similar gang on the other
s'dc to find holes in these objections,
and technical blunders in the techni-
calities. This is the highest art of
the legal profession, and the most expen-
sive brigand in the great army of thieves is
the one who can most successfully blast the
cause of justice and equity on some point
concerning the date at which some useless
document was put on a tile in an equally use-
less public office, and left there, and for-
gotten. And this art exists solely because
of the existence of the lawyer, for even (he
most obtuse of Judges if he is morally
qualified to be a Judge —can generally strike
a fair average as to the meaning of any
statute upon his own responsibility ; thcre-
fore, the business of the talented barrister is
to prevent him discovering that meaning,
and to howl him into a state of judicial
lunacy.. And by virtue of this same art,
other lawyers draft new Acts, and pile up
mountains upon mountains of verbiage, not
to define what the law is, but to prevent it
being defined as the thousand and one things
that it is not. Consequently Acts are made
more unfathomable and incomprehensible
in the effort to baffle and suppress the
lawyer, and through this same incomprelxen-
siblencss it is necessary to hire lawyers to
define their meaning before they arc of the
slightest use to the community at large.
The situation of the legil practitioner would
be identical with that of the rabbit if the
country had to buy an enormously expen-
sive rabbit in the first place, and a costly
fence to keep him out in the second, and
then to keep on buying more rabbits and
more fences to all eternity.
# # *
Jn the administration of criminal law the
lawyer certainly looks a shade less useless and
noxious than in the civil courts, but even
there he is entirely superfluous. It is un-
doubtedly a noble anti laudable thing to
defend those who are unjustly accused, but
it is only the ideal practitioner who does
this ; the real or garden variety of advocate
exists for quite a different purpose. Criminal
law depends less upon technicalities and the
elaborate obfuscation of the code, and much
more upon questions of actual fact, than
does the civil variety ; and the business of
each lawyer is to pervert the facts to the
last extremity; and each side requires a
lawyer simply because its own facts arc
being perverted by the man in the opposite
interest, therefore it becomes necessary to
hire somebody to pervert his facts by way of
retaliation. If no lawyers existed, then it
is only fair to assume that the facts on either
side would stand a much better show than
they do under existing conditions. Mean-
while the party which can afford to pay the
best perverter prospers, and the party
whose poverty compels him to be satisfied
with a cheap and inefficient perverter is
hanged : and the individual whom the law
gets hold of is not the one who is guilty,
but the one who has lacked the means to
engage an able and experienced falsi-
fier. Very often, of course, it gets the
real criminal, but that is a mere coincidence ;
it does not get him because of his criminality,
but because he had a cheap lawyer or none
at all And here again, the lawyer creates
the demand for his own existence, and if he
didn’t exist there would be no demand at
all.
# # *
Apart from those services the lawyer
answers a few other purposes ( f more or less
importance. He does a good business as a
conveyancer, because he has so elaborately
compounded the statutes (hat no one can
buy or sell land without hiring an expensive
professional individual to show him how to
do it. The conveyance of land is not neces-
sarily any more complex than the convey-
ance of sugar done up in a brown paper
bag, bub having successfully covered the
operati u with a veil of profound mystery,
he makes a handsome profit thereby. Sooner
or later, however, the world will abolish
lawyers and the statutes of their construc-
tion, and in so doing will simplify the pro-
cess of land-transfer till the conveyancer is
unnecessary. And then, as the natural con-
comitant of tliis reform, it will abolish the
whole system of private property in land,
and land-transfers will become extinct,
because no one will possess any land to
transfer. Also, the legal brotherhood sup-
plies advisers to the Government, for no
Government can find its way among the
maze of its own lawyer-built Acts of Par-
liament without professional aid, or
understand i s own meaning without expen-
sive outside assistance ; it acts as general
interpreter of sewage and ash-cart ordinances
to municipal bodies, for even the bye laws
concerning an ash-cart are too i rofound
for the ordinary lay comprehension ;
and it steers joint - stock companies
through the devious ways in which they can
clean out the general public without getting
into gaol. Further, it supplies occupants
for the bench, which is a most essential
function, not because legal training is
necessary for the administration of justice,
but because the Judge has to deal with laws
that are written in a legal jargon of unspeak-
able muddlement, and to strike a legal
average between the barrister who is lying
for the prosecution and the barrister who is
not telling the truth for the defence. The
lawyer makes the Judge—as that individual
is now understood—an essential element of
the legal machinery. If the lawyer were
abolished, and the statutes which he has
constructed replaced by a code written in
plain and simple English, then the sole
business of the Judge would be to
administer even-handed justice instead
of wading through a jungle of tangled
technicalities, and the judicial office
could be efficiently filled by any man en-
dowed with liberal views, a logical brain,
and a rcasonably-widc knowledge of the
world in general. And here again the
lawyer is the cause of himself, and makes
the demand for his own existence. The
world does not want him in any sense what-
ever. He only wants himself, and makes
the world pay the bill, and when the world
begins to realise the situation it will pay the
hill no longer, and in the great host of birds
of prey there will he one ravenous cormorant
the less.
The Matrimonial Partnership.
Sir Alfred Stephen in a recent letter to the
S.M. Herald, under the heading of “ Wives
and their Friends,” ((notes a case lately decided
in England, which throws a peculiar light
on the position held by married women accord-
ing to English law
According to English law, a wife could hriupr no action
for injuries done to her, without his (the husband's) con-
s nit; the redress being given, not to her, but to him. if
the husband was the wrongdoer, ndress was almost im-
possible. As a case illustrative of the complete helpless-
ness of women in England only a few years ago, I submit
the following; —lt was a case in which a woman bad boon
deserted for many years by her husband, who had gone to
America. She was a working woman, and just gained
enough to live upon, and to bring up a family of
small children whom the husband had left on
her hands, She met with a railway accident and
was seriously injured. When she attempted to bring an
action for damages against the railway company, the
company successfully defended it and obtained a non-
suit oh the ground that, being a married woman, she
couid not sue for damages for any wrong that had been
done to her. Someone took up her case, and after much
difficulty found the husband in America, and induced him
to allow himself to be joined in the suit with his wife, so
that she could sue the railway company. As it was shown
that she was incapacitated for the rest of her life from
earning her livelihood, the railway company was ordered
to pay £lOOO damages. This was not, of course, a very
large sum ; but it was sufficient to have kept the unfor-
tunate woman out of the workhouse. But the husband
had been too well informed of his and ids wife's legal
position. When the £lOOO came (o be paid, lie held out
Ids band for the cheque. It was necessarily paid to Idm
tlie law so ordered it in compensation for the damage
done to Ids property. With that money he rcinnud lo
America, leaving the woman who had suffered the damage
to go into the workhouse, without a penny to support
her.
# * #
This quotation is eloquent regarding the
position of women as Property according to the
reading of English law by the leading publicists
of the English law-courts themselves. Charles
Letourneau, in his admirable monograph on
“The Evolution of Marriage and of the
Family,” is at especial pains to collect evidence
from among savage tribes and rudimentary
societies proving the origin of all marriage in a
purchase of merchantable property, and the
utter impossibility of the primitive wife having
any concern in her own interests, or any pro-
perty of her own whatever. According to the
above quotation, however, M. Letourneau
could have amassed all his examples, not from
the practices and customs of savage and prehis-
toric tribes, but from the existing statute-books
of the most civilised peoples of Europe. As the
marriage and divorce laws of the greater part
of the most advanced of modern nations now
stand, the position of the wife is little better
than that of any other property —a thing owned,
having no share in her husband’s rights save
the share of a serf or of a servant. The worst
part of it all is that there is no attempt put
forth by law-makers to better woman’s position
as a wife on a broad and logical basis of justice.
Attempts there arc to give to women the fran-
chise, to open the University to female students,
to broaden the avenues to divorce. But divorce
is in itself only a pulling down ; it is not a
building up.
* # #
Is not all this an attempt at re-adjustment at
the wrong end? Why should not the conditions
of marriage be more equitable ? There are two
ways of doing this, and they are not difficult to
seek. The first way is one which would bo
tolerated by no civilised community, simply
because it would mean a return to free love,
promiscuity, and barbarism. It would mean
the denial of paternity, the non-recognition and
the non-intervention of the State in all conjugal
contracts, the refusal of the responsibilities of
heredity, and the abolition of bastardy and the
bcqueathal of property by descent. The other
way is plain, logical, and just. Let the State
discard all sentimentality in the matter and
consider the marital union as a purely business
contract, like any other form of commercial
partnership. What does a woman gain
financially, say, by divorce from a man
who is consigned to gaol for some offence
against the laws of his country ? When the
man has fulfilled his term of punishment he
cannot reclaim her —that is all. Put it on
another basis. Regard bis committal to prison
as compulsory sequestration of a legal partner
in an estate. Wind up bis business and pay
over the just share in such business to his
partner, who may lie hereafter properly regarded
as his widow. Let the terms of every marital
contract be accurately set forth in a legal docu-
ment which shall stand in law as a genuine
deed of partnership, and in which the rights of
the wife shall be absolutely assured. A writer
in one of the English magazines recently pro-
posed a method by which all forms of brcach-
of-promise of marriage should be settled by a
similar means. . He suggested that the contract-
ing parties should sign bonds pledging them-
selves to observe the terms of the instru-
ment, Subject to certain conditions which should
constitute just grounds of evasion of fulfilment,
under a penalty of so much money ; the deed to
be duly witnessed as all other business agree-
ments are. This, in the writer’s opinion, would
abolish at one stroke the whole work of the
Courts in the matter of breaeh-of-promise
cases. Apply this system also to marriage
itself. Secure the position of the wife against
the husband as a business partner is secured,
and a case similar to that instanced by Sir
Alfred Stephen would not only be improbable
—it would be impossible.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
"One Pirst-Class Policeman!"
A few days ago a man (whoso name, as he was
acquitted, there is no reason to give) was
charged in a Sydney court with attempted
stealing. It was only “attempted conse-
quently there were no material evidences hi
prisoner’s possession to support the charge. The
case consisted of one policeman who said that
the prisoner had been trying to steal, and one
prisoner who said that he hadn’t. The jury
gave the alleged offender the benefit of the
doubt, and Judge M‘Faeland, who seemed
almost petrified with amazement, received the
verdict with this memorable observation ;
Not guilty ! Is that really your verdict, gentlemen,
after lira i inn the evidence of a first-class constable f


The inference to be drawn from this is that the
evidence of one whole and complete policeman
is expected to have a paralysing effect on a
buy, and to swamp all outside statements,
just here it seems to be a serious pity that the
Bible wasn’t written by a first-class constable,
so as to suppress criticism. The average
Judge —and most Judges are very average
individuals —will go the whole judicial hog on
the testimony of one “cop,” and evidently
looks on that individual as something more than
human. Yet the “cop” differs from the ordinary
citizen only in Ids clothes, and as it is the man
that swears, and not the clothes, his testimony
is just the testimony of an ordinary human
being after all. He is selected chiefly on account
of his dimensions, and there is no reason at all
to suppose that a large man is any more reliable
than a small one. In the prisoner’s case it was
the unsupported statement of one constable
against the equally unsupported statement of
one prisoner. The fact that the latter teas a
prisoner doesn’t necessarily invalidate his testi-
mony ; anybody is a prisoner as soon as an
officer seizes him, and an officer may seize any-
body-—he may even grab Parkes off his veran-
dah if he is a new officer, and knows no better.
Moreover, every man is assumed to be innocent
until proved guilty, and, therefore, by all
the canons of law, it was merely a case
in which one innocent citizen swore to a
certain statement and another innocent citizen
contradicted him. The prisoner had every reason
to swear as he did for his liberty was at stake.
The officer also might have had —we make no
personal allegation —reason to swear as he did,
for —despite all theories to the contrary —a
policeman’s promotion depends upon his effici-
ency, and an efficient policeman is a policeman
who secures convictions. There is absolutely
no reason to assume that this particular officer
was giving false evidence ; but the one point
for the jury to consider, was that the constable
and the prisoner had exactly the same reasons
for swearing truly, and also for swearing falsely.
And, in addition to all this, there was the
further chance that the policeman had drawn a
wrong inference; there was no evidence of
theft —merely an assumption, which might have
been incorrect, that theft was intended. The
Jury gave the prisoner the benefit of the doubt
—no honest jury could have done otherwise—
and then Judge M'Farland jumped at them
with both judicial feet —not upon the facts of
the case, but for not being paralysed at the
sight of “ a first-class constable. ” And as they
were engaged to consider the evidence, and not
to be paralysed by any constable, this was
eminently unreasonable in every sense.
Divorce by Mutual Consent.
At the present moment there are confined in
Darlinghurst Prison, Sydney, 20 individuals
charged with wife-desertion, and two charged
with wife-and-child desertion, making 22 in all.
These may be regarded asbut a small part of a very
large accumulation, for the absconding husbands
who have paid up the amounts demanded by
Saw for the support of their wives are at liberty;
and so are the individuals who have not paid up,
but have gone a-missing and can’t be found;
and those who have not paid up and can be
found, but against whom their wives have taken
no further proceedings. Also, the list does not
include those against whom no proceedings were
taken at all, because they vanished completely
at the very outset, or for other reasons ; and,
further, it only includes male absconders, and
of escaped wives there is no record whatever.
But with these five exceptions, covering, most
probably, at least 90 per cent, of the total, the 22
prisoners in Darlinghurst represent fairly well
the last few weeks’ business in this particular
department of crime in a certain limited area
around Sydney ; and if the prisoners in othe r
gaols are also taken into account, and the total
is multiplied by three or four, a fair idea may
be arrived at of the number of marriages that
have been unofficially cancelled within a brief
period in N.S. Wales. Practically, these are all
■cases of divorce, only the slow and melancholy
Law refuses to understand the fact. The essence
<of marriage is the union of two individuals,
■and when these two individuals are permanently
disunited, then the marriage is cancelled. The
mere circumstance that the statute fails to recog-
nise the situation, has no possible bearing on
the case. Under a system of divorce by mutual
consent, no doubt the larger proportion of the
unhappy marriages which have concluded so
disastrously in Darlinghurst and elsewhere
would long since have been obliterated, to the
.great relief of both parties concerned ; for the
absconding husband, by the mere act of abscond-
ing, may be reasonably held as signifying his
desire to be set free, and the deserted wife, in a
very large number of cases, would gladly get
rid of what is merely an unprofitable incubus.
But neither Shiels of Victoria nor Jawbone
Neild of New South Wales—the twin apostles
of progress in this direction—have yet dared to
suggest that divorce should be permitted merely
for the reason that both parties want it. It is
difficult to imagine a better reason ; if the only
two individuals concerned desire to be set free
from each other it is their own affair, and
nobody else has any possible right to interfere ;
mid if divorce cannot be allowed because
everybody interested is anxious to have it, it is
difficult to imagine any set of conditions
under which a sane law-maker would allow it.
Nevertheless, it is held by every Australian
Court that “ collusion ” is a sufficient reason
why a decree of separation should be refused ;
in other words, when the Judge suspects that
the two individuals concerned are both agree-
able, he at once rejects the petition from legally
authorised cussedness ; but if one of the parties
objects strongly, then he goes ahead from pure
contrariness. His avowed reason for doing
this is that divorce by consent is immoral—
though why it is specially immoral has never
yet been explained. s> And apparently both
flie Judge and the Legislature entirely
fail to see that it doesn’t matter in any
way—so far as the court is concerned—whether
the system is immoral or not. It exists with-
out the recognition of the law; if the law
recognised it, it couldn’t exist any more than it
does already. By refusing to recognise it the
law may force both divorced parties into sub-
sequent immorality, but beyond that it can do
nothing. Divorce by mutual consent practi-
cally exists in several of the States of America,
and the great army of orthodoxists point woe-
fully to the vast array of judicial separations
which this nefarious law brings to pass. Yet,
after all, separations are as plentiful in the
moral land of Australia—the only difference is
that the law refuses to register them. And in
like manner the law might refuse to register
deaths, but people would die just the same, and
Australia would be none the healthier by virtue
of that non-registration. To argue against an
accomplished fact by refusing to put it down
on paper is worse even than the proceeding of
the ostrich, which refuses to recognise its
pursuer’s existence by hiding its head in the
sand. For in this case the Law goes one
better, by denying that there is any ostrich or
any sand at all.
In Liquidation,
At the present moment a vast array of old
banks, caved-in building-societies and exploded
financial institutions, scattered over all parts of
Australia, Tasmania and Maoriland, are
struggling through the slow process of liquida-
tion. How many there are it is impossible to
count, for many of them started liquidating in
the days of ancient history, and their names are
all but forgotten, and the shareholders have
faded away, and the creditors are buried or
burst, and yet the long, long process of
liquidating keeps going on for ever. It is the
dreariest process that ever was invented in all
the moral wastes of commerce. When a public
institution bursts up, it always bursts with a
substantial balance to the good—on paper, at
all events; and there is the inevitable
assurance that the creditors will be paid in full,
and something will remain over for the share-
holders. Then a pompous accountant or finan-
cier, whose stomach alone suggests solidity and
financial responsibility, is appointed to wind
things up at a handsome salary, paid regularly;
and he rents an expensive office, and hires
most of his friends and relatives to
help him liquidate. Then it is discovered that
the market is unfavourable, and the properties
can’t be disposed of till better times arrive, and
while he is waiting for these better times the
staff still sits around, and draws its salary, and
liquidates. Ponderous reports are drawn up
at an extra remuneration of £2 an hour or there-
abouts, and then an expensive hall is hired for
these reports to be read in. A high-priced
solicitor and an expensive barrister start to
argue before a costly Judge about the “ list of
contributories,” and then they have sundry
appeals over the matter, and at intervals the
ease is “ mentioned ” in court. The assets fade
away in postage - stamps, gas, office - rent,
salaries, tips, commissions, repairs to empty
houses, more commissions to the agent who
doesn’t let the empty houses, advertising, taxes,
legal expenses through hiring a costly solicitor
to write to each man who owes 2s. fid., legal
expenses perusing the reply in which he repudi-
ates the 2s. fid., more legal expenses through
the expensive barrister suing for the 2s. fid.,
court fees, &c., through the case being
again “ mentioned,” and still more barristers
and solicitors and court fees to discover
whether it comes “within the meaning of
the Act. ” Then there are cross-actions because
some other defunct company bought a three-
cornered swamp, and mortgaged it to the bank,
and then sold it to this defunct company sub-
ject to the mortgage, and the bank sold the
mortgage to a three-ball Hebrew, and the com-
pany sold the land to some swindled labourer
on time-payment and couldn’t give him a title,
and the Hebrew went for the labourer, and
chaos ensued. Also, there are calls on the
shareholders to pay the liquidator’s screw and
the law expenses, and some of them bolt, and
some of them file, and the rest hire a whole
gang of barristers, and talk each other to death
in court. Meanwhile, the liquidation doesn’t
go on worth a cent, and many an institution
which started to wind up eight years ago hasn’t
even begun to make any progress yet. In fact,
the whole system is a delusion and an unspeak-
able fraud. It never produces any dividends ;
if the assets of the dead institution amount to
£10,000 and its liabilities are sixpence, the one
absolute certainty is that it will cost the
whole £10,000 to find out, by direction
of the Full Court, how to pay the sixpence.
The only short way out of the difficulty is to
trade off the properties of defunct banks and
building societies by means of lotteries, and
abolish the liquidator altogether—to raffle the
swamps, and allotments, and house properties,
and offices, and to put in the office furniture
and the bad debts as minor prizes. This
scheme would at least wind things up promptly,
and produce something in the way of hard cash,
and no other scheme yet invented has done
either. Also, it would be 16 times more moral
and less demoralising than horse-racing and
bookmaking; it would avert ruin from hundreds
of exhausted shareholders; and it would
furnish a cheap, profitable, and benevolent
recreation for the people ; and when the
creditors and shareholders were all paid any
surplus could be judiciously annexed by the
State, whereby the device would be good for
everybody and a beneficent thing all round.
And these should be the only lotteries per-
mitted by the State.
The pressure of public opinion is telling on the
Playford Government in South Australia.
Last year they displaced Dr. Cockburn’s
Cabinet on the ground that his progressive
land-tax was going too far and interfering with
the recognised rights of property, and they pro-
mised, if they got office, to keep things calm
and steady. Since then the increase of the
large estates and the growth of public opinion
in favour of more taxation on the land have
been about equal, and, bowing to the storm,
the Playford Government have introduced
four Bills, which rip up the Sacred rights of
property from the very foundations, and scatter
them to the winds in small and unrecognisable
fragments. They propose a tax of IJd. in the
pound on estates over £15,000 ; to give tenants
the right to turn their leases into freeholds
without the consent of the landlords ; to levy
stiff progressive legacy-duties, and to limit the
power of a man leaving landed property by will.
Up to the present the House of Assembly has
been so much stunned that it has only expressed
its surprise by inarticulate interjections, but
the liberal members will soon wake up to the
fact that the Bills are so drawn as to provide
that all these great changes are to take place
when everybody now living is thoroughly dead,
and therefore they are not calculated to do
much good to the present generation beyond
enabling the Playford gang to stop the agita-
tion for Cockburn’s progressive tax.
“A Loyal Australian,” who has evidently
spent many years in India, and thus speaks
from experience, writes to curse The Bulletin
hard in re an article entitled “ The Two
Governors-General.” The purpose of that
article was to use strong language about the
tyranny of Anglo-Indian officials in muzzling
the native press, and prosecuting native writers
who criticise British rule, and it alluded to this
state of things as “lop-sided justice.” Then
the “ Loyal Australian ” comes down heavily as
follows:
Lop-sided justice—good heavens ! Is the author of this
aware that every ofliuer administering justice in India
must have passed, at 23 years of age alnut, no loss than
three competitive examinations, the intense rigour of
which is without parallel in any country out of China?
For the first one, at 21, which is open to any legitimately
born subject of the empire, he must be able to stand the
most searching test human ingenuity can contrive of his
acquaintance with one dead language, and one or more
modern ditto. This has an.optional aspect, of course, but
one language besides his own, dead or alive, he has to be
011 the friendliest terms with, or fail. Then in mathe-
matics he has to foot every rung of that brain-crazing
mental exercise, besides which having to know Sanskrit
and become a walking Cyclopaedia as to such airy trifles
as English history from the Conquest to this year of
grace, and the same country’s literature as far back as
Chaucer, &c.
The Bulletin briefly replies to this prize idiot
that it doesn’t care a straw even if the boss
Anglo-Indian knows Choctaw, ancient Phoe-
nician, Eskimo metaphysics, and all the
languages that were spoken before the deluge.
Nero was a whale for knowledge, so were
Henry VIII., Amurath the Terrible, and half
the vilest scoundrels of history. The argu-
ment has no more bearing on the case than it
has upon the polar bear. Besides, if there is no
injustice in India because the civil servant has
to pass a stiff examination, then there must be
still less injustice—if that were possible—in
China, where, as the “ loyal Australian ”
admits, the examination is still stiffer.
Chinese “justice” is notoriously corrupt and
hideously brutal, which clearly proves that
equity doesn’t come out of competitive exams.
The writer then goes on to point to the
railways, waterworks, colleges, missionaries,
and the countless other benefits which
Britain has conferred on India. We
don’t publish this part of his letter, for we
admit the whole lot, railways, missionaries, and
all. And the entire accumulation have no con-
nection at all with the subject under discussion.
A Government may make 20,000,000 miles of
railway, and may fill all the land with mis-
sionaries till you fall over a preacher at every
corner, and every mud-pie that is thrown in the
whole empire hits some expounder in bis meek
and shining eye, but if that same Government
lias to muzzle the press then it is a Government
which can’t bear criticism. And even 20,000,000
more miles of railway and a thousand civil
service examinations can’t alter this infallible
inference in the least.
One of the urgent requirements of the demo-
cracy is the periodical election of magistrates
and Judges by the people. At stated intervals
—say once in every three years at the utmost—
the nation would then have an opportunity of
getting rid of the fools and incurable bigots, the
aggressive, hide-bound loyalists to a foreign
country, the “ hanging Judges,” the eccentric
idiots who pass ten-year sentences for petty
larceny, and the brutal bullies in horse-hair who
browbeat juries into returning verdicts on in-
sufficient evidence, all of whom are now almost
irremovable, for they can only be shifted by
death, or by the amputation of both legs. Also
there would then be a chance to get rid, by
some other process save that of old age and
senility, of the country Justice who knows no
law, and the lopsided, pig-headed city beak
who sends up the homeless citizen for 12 months
because he has no visible means of support, and
fines the person who has trodden on his wife in
the sum of 10s., with the alternative of
seven days. And further, it would pro-
vide a means of shunting the judicial
humourist with his imbecile grin, and the
judicial hyena with his howl and his bloodshot
eye, and the judicial drunk with his incoherent
notes and his general inability to tell which
individual is the prisoner and which is the
prosecuting counsel; likewise the Judge, who
owes money to both the litigant Hebrews before
him, and also to their attorneys, and to the
usurers of the city unto seventy times seven.
Lastly, and more important than all, it would
enable the people to cast out the judicial
snuffiebuster and to bounce the aggressive
Christian off the Bench. Appointing a man as
a J udge for life is as great a farce as nominating
a man to the Legislative Council for life. No
one can ever tell before a man is elevated to
the Bench what particular kind of monomaniac
he will prove to be —he is simply shunted on to
bis judicial seat as an irreparable, life-long
experiment. And, therefore, the whole system
is an outrage on democratic principles—an
insult to the common-sense of the nation—a
miserable foolishness —a perversion of justice,
and a dead failure. To appoint any man for
life to any particular post is only one remove in
its idiocy from appointing a dynasty to rule a
country.
“ Toowoomba ” writes to The Bulletin to ask
the old, old, weary conundrum—whether is
Protection or the Single-tax the better policy
for a new,country ? This is the same miserable
problem which turns up for over ami ever. The
average faddist can never hold more than one
fad at a time ; he never will realise that no
country can live on one nostrum only. If the
question were altered to read: “Is it best to
wear boots or a shirt?” he would probably
realise the situation, and reply that he wanted
both of them, and some other clothes as well,
but he never by any chance applies the same
rule to politics. And in any case the “Single-tax”
is drivelling insanity, for if Australia adopted
a “ Single-tax ” it would thereby prohibit itself
taxing Chinamen to keep down the Mongolian
influx, or taxing dogs to restrict the multiplica-
tion of mongrels, and it would make grog as
cheap as milk. The term Land Nationalisa-
tion was turned into the term “ Single-tax” by
a mendacious Foreign-trade party in order to
bolster up the lying legend that no Protec-
tionist can be an enemy of landlordism.
What anew country wants is Land Nationalisa-
tion, to ensure that the national wealth
shall be divided on an equitable basis, and Pro-
tection to make certain that there will be any
wealth to divide. Also, it wants Independence,
as a guarantee that the fruits of Protection and
Land Nationalisation shallnot both be shrivelled
up in some British quarrel; and it wants good
liquor, lest the rising nation should be poisoned
before it has time to realise any of the advan-
tages of independence ; and it also wants educa
tion and One-man-one-vote, so that it may b<
able to prevent the resurrection of monopoly ir
future days; and decentralisation, and th
abolition of Chinamen, and other things a i
well. But it doesn’t want to live on any single
dogma, and the Foreign-traders, and Single-tax
faddists who preach Land Nationalisation as a
doctrine by itself, and the other men who
preach teetotalism or republicanism or Protec-
tion as the whole duty of man, are a joint-stock
delusion and a snare. “One man one fad ”is a
ruinous doctrine.
Sample of loyalism from an article on “ Wales”
in the Review of Reviews :
In these last days many men have talked glibly about a
republic. Such words are lightly spoken, but those who
utter them have little weighed their significance. No
such breach in the historical continuity of our Constitu-
tion should be spoken of save with hated breath as men
speak of a possible catastrophe—which may Heaven in
mercy avert. . . . Until a republic has been created
which ventures to elect a female President any subversion
of the monarchy would be a disaster to progress. It
would be better—much better —to confine the succession
to women. We have always done well under female
sovereigns.
The moral seems to be that the great advantage
of royalty is “continuity.” Continuity is the
virtue of hanging out a desperately long time ;
it also implies extreme length, after the manner
of a snake, or a bill of costs, or a protracted
lagoon, or anything else that lias a great area
of remoteness between its head and its tail. A
chronic bore, a dynasty, an hereditary disease,
and a sea-serpent are all continuous, and so is
the interest .charged at Ike pawnshop. Taking
it all round, continuity seems a cloubttul virtue,
and the idea of a line of female sovereigns is
still more doubtful. If the males of a royal
family are all too scrofulous, stupid, and
obnoxious to be trusted in a public billet, their
mothers and daughters must be a desperately
shady lot. England lias speculated in four of
them, anyhow ; and one of them burned Pro-
testants, and another burned Catholics, and the
third was more dense than the ordinary cow,
and the fourth is a money-grubbing nonentity.
And if the country has done well “ under ”
them, they have all done remarkably well on
top of the country. For, however the corpse
underneath may prosper, the cabbage that
grows on top generally prospers a good deal
more.
A correspondent sends us some particulars
about a large New South Wales public company
which seems to have got the art of monopoly
down pretty fine. It employs about 300 miners,
and the works are situated in a lonely basin
among the bills, about 15 miles from anywhere.
The company runs its own grocery store,
butchery, bakery, and rum-shop, and charges a
stiff percentage above normal prices. The
trade, however, is a monopoly ; the bowelless
corporation owns the land for miles around, and
allows no honest competitor on its territory ;
consequently it does the whole business.
Their are no amusements in the place barring
the company’s rum-shop, which leads to lavish
drinking; and thanks to this and the manner
in which the agony is piled up in the matter
of prices the men soon fall into arrears. Then
they are required to sign an agreement whereby
their bills are deducted from their wages.
Presently they have no wages at all; they toil
incessantly, but the debts always keep a little
ahead, and life consists entirely of a weary
round of credit victuals and unpaid-for liquor.
They can’t go away, for they possess not a
single cent. They can’t strike, for they have no
money, and the instant they struck the supply
of provisions would stop. They can’t belong to
a trades-union, for in that case also the supply
of food would be knocked off". They are hope-
less exiles, like those of Siberia or Sagbalien ;
and unless some revolutionary force rushes their
valley some day, and destroys the bills, and
sacks the store and the bakery and the grog-
shop, the situation seems incurable. It is the
same old story. The monopolist is the same kind
of monopolist in all lands and in all ages. He is
a nigger-driver, with a Bible in one hand and
his overgrown stomach in the other, and all he
wants is a fair show.
The Bulletin recently referred to the present
Australian police “ move-on ” system so far as
released prisoners are concerned. Says Com-
missioner Petekswalo (S.A.) in his last
report:—
Influx of Criminals.
1 am sorry to say that we are still having a large
addition to our criminal population from the eastern
colonies, to check which I have been obliged to put in
force the Convicts Prevention Act, No. 140, of 1879, by
which any person found in this colony within three years
from the date of the expiration of a sentence which he
has served in any other colony is liable to be imprisoned
for two years. This Act, which is also in force in Victoria,
is liberally acted on there and necessitates an equal
strictness here.
Now that S. Australia cries “move-on ” what
are the helpless unfortunates to do ? Either
starve or relapse into crime ! The police are
ever behind them, and the gaol-door yawns
ahead.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Sept. 2,1891.
My Dear Moorabinda,—
The first vice-regal flight into the regions of amateur
theatricals occurred on 27th ult. on a very realistic stage
erected in the red drawing-room, the rest of which, with
the ball-room, was crowded with a full-dress throng. As
there wasn’t one candid gallery boy present, the applause
was uninterrupted—and though unqualified rapture at
the performance of the purely Government House troupe
was only to be expected, still it is soothing to know that
everything really went off very well. The curtain-raiser
was a Fairy tale, “Creatures of Impulse,” in which the
olive-branches of the house of Jersey cavorted with con-
siderable aplomb—the younsrest daughter looking very
pretty and fairylike, after which came “ Little Toddle-
kins,” w'herein Secretary Goschen as a bouncing, golden-
wigged damsel in a turkey-red skirt and jacket over a
blue blouse, small, perched sailor hat and ecru parasol
and fan, was very near to nature-on-a-public-holiday ; his
love-passages with Chumley, at the finish, let the curtain
down to shrieks of laughter from the guests, who then
dispersed in search of the refreshment buffet. The dress-
ing was sui generis, Lady J ersey (who acted as promptness
to the players and did not appear till “all was ended
comme il faut”) wore ivory-white silk draped with black
lace ; Mrs. Fitzgerald (a guest at Government House), a
very fetching black lace and silk costume ; and another
striking costume was a black silk gown, the bodice
covered with white chiffon frills and puffed sleeves of
while chiffon to the knuckles—worn by Mrs. Yandeveere
Greene,
What surprises me most about the sassiety girl is the
artless way in which she attacks the affable Government
House youth with wreathed smiles and outstretched
hands, while that flattered five-feet-odd of old noblesse
suffers her onslaught with a calm resignation beautiful to
witness. Thu common sort of young man has to arrange
all the sorties himself and be torn with doubts as to
whether she is really going to speak to him or not, but
the weary vice-regal attache only screws his pane deeper
into his eye-socket, emits a surprised “haw,” and pre-
pares to receive cavalry. Only the other day a society
girl and a very pretty one, too—caught a flying aide-de-
camp by laying a tiny little gloved hand on his arm for
about ten seconds by Shrewsbury’s clock.
Ladies who go to England with pet nuisances, in the
shape of poodle dogs, in tow, ought to beware of the
second mate of a certain mail-steamer. On a recent
voyage out, he took a violent dislike to the four-legged
darling of one of the lady passengers, and one night, as
the cold stars twinkled and nobody was on deck save this
abandoned villain, ill-fated Toby, in all the splendour of
his velvet jacket, forged along the poop, wagging his
pampered tail. . . A horrible splash finishes the story
told by the ship’s doctor, who had Toby’s mistress on his
hands with hysterical fits for the rest of the journey I
In Sydney, by the way, the dog mania has given way to
the infant craze. Every girl who has a little sister gets
her up a la Kate Greenaway and sets her trotting down
the Block, and the amount of solid exercise dressable
infants get at present is enough to give them bow-legs.
It amuses me dreadfully sometimes—in ball-rooms and
dress-circles- to quiz the honest soul who won’t put on a
well-ventilated bodice for worlds, yet arrays herself in a
more flagrant manner than her V-shaped contemporary
dare do. I mean the woman who puts on a sleeveless
bodice in some dark colour, hacked down to the waist-
line fore and aft; under this she wears a pink-on-white,
tight-fitting bodice with sleeves (you must understand
there isn’t a bare inch of her showing !), and then she sits
in the front row with a smile of conscious virtue and half
the opera-glasses of the house focussed on her to see if it
really is what it looks like. And they don’t restrict the
originality of their devices to their jackets. At a big
dance the other evening there rotated a lady in black
gauze skirt with a frontal design of two long bands o
white-pink satin from hip to hem joining the curved pink
satin waistcoat at the top, with a strip of black tulle
betwixt. It was a splendid suggestion of what she might
have looked like as a pre-Adamite female clothed chrono-
logically.
Judged as a winter flower-show held on the verge of
spring the so-called “Camelia Show,” held in Sydney
Town Hall, on 26th ultimo., deserves nothing but praise,
though one cannot quite understand why the Horticul-
tural Society should rush a show along when flowers are
scarce and unreliable and the spring exhibition only a
few weeks ahead. The Governor, in a large hat attended
by Goschen in a small one, attended (for his part) by two
young ladies in feather boas, formally inaugurated the
floralities, but only about four people responded to the
appeal for three cheers for Lord Jersey, and the opening
was rather lame and impotent because Lady J. wasn’t
there. As a lady beside me remarked, “She expected to
see more kamraillas,” and so did I, but there were lots of
things to make up for them—the first prize decorated
table in pink roses and ferns, for instance, also the dis-
play of indigenous flora—the latter only as a novelty,
mind you—because native flowers are too eccentric in
shape, colour, and scent, to be wortli gushing over. I
always seem to see in them Nature's excuse for the
colouring and texture of our women. The centre
bouquet, on the wild-flower table (sent per Mr. Goschen
with compliments to Lady Jersey), was as neatly arranged
as such a collection of bristles and spikes could ever hope
to be, but it wasn’t quite fair to stud it over with English
buttercups.
The crowd at the Camelia Show was largely feminine.
The most charming woman there was Olga Nethersole,
rather tragically goltcn-up in a black visile, edged with
black feathers, and a littic, lid shaped black bonnet on
her carefully-dressed locks, quite a relief from the usual
daylight professional, with its pink cheek gleaming
through the shrouding spotted fall. Miss Nethersole
wore no verraillion and no veil, and the result was some-
thing “chic.” But I didn’t like a mastic-coloured Tudor
cape, with a yoke studded with glass “ diamonds”—it
looked as if the wearer had taken a header into a broken-
bottle heap. '■
By the way, on the opening day of the show, at least
two well-known free feeders were put in for it unex-
pectedly. You see, an enterprising caterer had strung a
buffet across the vestibule, and these two ghouls thought
it was a delicate little committee attention and let them-
selves go according!}'. However, at the fourteenth spear-
ing of their little forks a waiter grasped the situation and
murmured “Tvvo-and-six, please,” and the ghouls rose up
with freezing dignity and expressed their disgust in
voices choked with custard-roll, and proudly paid the
reckoning with a scathing comment on the age of the
ham-sandwiches and a biting allusion to the tricks of
tradespeople in general.
The fair in aid of that praiseworthy institution, St.
Vincent’s Hospital, opened to bad business in the big
Town Hall (it didn’t look so big with all the draped stalls
about) on 31st ult., with Cardinal Moran in his red moire
polonaise (I hope he won’t excommunicate me if I hint
that it is getting a bit shiny on the shoulders) as chief
spokesman, seconded by Lord and Lady Jersey and ex-
Mayor Riley. Lady Jersey opened the Fair with a rather
florid oration, and rounded off with poetry, and was pain-
fully nervous during the ordeal, and Lord Jersey supple-
mented the “ Kyardinal’s ” deliberate statements with a
speech that the crowd took to be waggish because the
speaker grinned so all the time. After which the guber-
natorial party (with one exception) went round and
bought largely at every stall—Lord Jersey buying a num-
ber of books for the cadet guard laid on in the building,
and presenting them personally to the boys, who are
thus made to cherish loyal sentiments at a vice-regal
outlay of eighteenpence per head. Lady Jersey also
shopped largely, but a shaven lordling, though in the
gubernatorial suite, certainly didn’t follow it. In vain did
bewchus damsels (there were lots of pretty girls about,
too - especially two at the flower-stall in the centre, and a
grey-eyed, persuasive being who was pushing a cane
furniture lottery over the way) button-hole him at every
turn. Not a red cent would the lordling yield rip, giving
as his excuse that “ he had no money and couldn’t borrow
any.” Coming from another man this would savour of
evasion—but coming from a lordling “ in a situation” it
was probably just the bald, dreary G. Washington, after
all.
There were all sorts of things in the bazaar—a “fish-
pond,” a spinning-Jenny (there were two of those around
the fair when you come to think of it!) and the usual
jumble of odds and ends that make the residuum of such
exhibitions. Music there was—enough to deafen one—if
they keep it up, ear-trumpets at a shilling a time would
show a big dividend at the finish, I predict, but the
“ National Anthem,” softly sung by the Turners, Mrs.
Vandeveere-Greene and Rainford, was an artistic effort.
The crowd was moderately large on half-crown day and
appeared to be keeping its wardrobe dark for the Spring
meeting at Randwick, though one or two arrested the
eye. Lady Jersey (who isn’t too “ intellectual ”to eschew
a neat waist) sported steel-grey brocade with front of
grey chiffon, bonnet in black and gold ; Mrs. W. P.
Manning (Mayoress), in heliotrope with a circle design
in white, bonnet in black and gold ; Miss Stephen, in
cocoa-brown and old blue ; the Misses Halliday wore
tailor-made frocks; Mrs. F. B. Freehill, copper silk
covered with black Tosca net and jetted, collar ; Annis
Montague looked well, togged cap-a-pie, in French grey ;
Mrs. S. A. Moss, in black, braided in black, large, grey-
hat with ostrich feathers ; and Mrs. Vandeveere-Greene,
in a gown the colour of her name, sported some special
furs. Other people present were Judge Fawcett, Captain
Leigh, Captain “Chumley,” Dr. Do Lambert, Mr
Calvert, Mayor Manning and any number of priests,
who, oddly enough, didn’t seem a bit nonplussed at the
wiles of the lottery syrens.
Harking back to Sara—are all the little agonised
verselets (mostly commencing “ Ah 1 that we had never
met ”) which are sent to the papers to be “ kindly trans-
lated’’out of bad English into poetic French, going to
wing their way o\ er the stormy seas to Sara ? Else why
this w hyness? But this is the sort of rhyme that would
fit the case like a glove
A leading comedian—an adorer of the departed, almost-
forgotten Sara—was the victim of a heartless jokelet,
the other day, after she sailed. He received a little
scented billet signed “ Sara Bernhardt.” assuring him of
her warm regard, and hoping to meet him ere long in
Paris. This prize he showed all round, and probably wore
next his heart at off-times, until somebody sadly explained
that an ordinary Sydney solicitor had pieced the French
together, signature and all, and the truth was not in it.
Judging from all one sees and hears, the safest thing a
money-hunter can do if he doesn’t wish to face the
divorce-court sooner or later, is to snare a real heiress,
because a rich woman is usually far more closely wedded
to her “ establishment” than she is to her husband, whom
she, as a rule, merely regards as a necessary adjunct to he r
entourage. So no matter how blank a spec, he turns out
to be, his wife will put up with him, partly for the look of
the thing, and partly because radical changes cost hard
cash—and the opulent female in the nodding bonnet is
always very careful to get the right change for a sovereign.
The uncertain woman to marry is the woman who has a
reasonable income with which she foots her own
bills, who has not to coax her lord for the wherewithal to
revel in a Spring bonnet, but signs her own cheques and
complains about the cooking and generally figures as a
boarder in the husband’s home. Independence of this
kind acts, to the dazzling youths who And Platonics
rather paying, as pollen to the bee, and the consequences
are often absorbing reading to the general public. So I
say to all men, if you will marry money—marry your
millionairess—when you catch her.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Sydney Liedertafel’s next shriek will be assisted by
forty-two boys’ voices, which have more unearthly music
in them than “grown-up ” lilts ever possess. No matter
if a chorister is the scourge of his neighbourhood per-
sonally, his warbling always sounds like an angel calling.
Sydney Owls hyginxed on 10th ult. at Needs’ rooms,
and made quite a success of it. As M.’sO. the “Gold-
finch” and his companion bird found partners for every-
body, and the sixteen dances were supplemented by
extras leading to a small hours breaking-up.
The ball given by Mrs. Merewether to “warm’’the
barn like newly-built Australian clubhouse was a little
stiff, as lots of the members’ wives came from the country
and didn’t know the female relatives of the town members
—and there you have it. Lord and Lady Jersey and suite
were present and the supper was an inspiration.
Mayor and Mayoress Manning gave an invitation “at
home ” on 24th ult. in their rooms at Sydney Town Hail
with music as the draw of the evening. Annis Montague
(settled in Sydney as a teacher) was amongst the song-
sters, and Madame Kellcrmarm contributed a polonaise.
A swift girl may go three-quarters of a mile in a waltz,
and 14 miles on a full card.
There was quite an Australian gathering at St. Jude’s
Church, South Kensington, London, on 30th June, on the
occasion of the marriage of Miss Mabel Hinny, younger
daughter of the late John Hinny, of Sydney, and niece of
Mr. “ Jemmy ” Johnson, the well-known Sydney solicitor.
The happy man was Dr. Mourilyan, of H.M.S. Immortahte.
The wedding was quite a show affair, and it was a subject
of general remark how very pretty all the Australian girls
looked, comparing more than favourably with their
English sisters. Amongst the Sydney people present
were Mrs. Thomas Mort, Mrs. C. T. Gedye, Mrs. Dalglish,
Miss de Lauret, Charley Lett and his pretty daughter,
Mrs. G. F. Want and her daughter, and last, but not
least, Lady Samuel, “ Our Ambassadress,” as she is now
called. Dr. Mourilyan, by the bye, is a cousin of Mourilyan,
of the Basilisk, who discovered the Northern harbour
which hears his name.

is that they haven’t got a return ticket.

A young English workman was, 11 years
ago, sentenced to 20 years for “shooting to
murder ’ his sweetheart, who had jilted him. On
her intercession he has been released, and she will
marry him.

It has rained at every Central Cumberland
(N.S.W.) election for the last twenty years.
Should the electors not take the hint, but keep on
returning such awful political creatures as Nobbs,
Dale and Co., the Lord will yet have to send fire
and brimstone, instead of rain.
Was it not apart of the contract that the
heavy and cumbrous Sydney Post-office pillars
should, for the public convenience, be connected
by telephone with the fire and police-staticns ? If
it was, then why is it that these conditions have
not been complied with up to date? Also, who
has worked the oracle, and how did he work it?
Mr. Fred. Brock, the well-known Sydney
stock-agent, who shot away both his eyes and the
bridge of his nose in an attempt at suicide, has
died of brain-inflammation. On recovering con-
sciousness after entering hospital he said to the
surgeon : “I am sorry I made such a mull of it.”
It is just as well that poor Brock did not recover,
for the hideousness of his injuries was almost
unparalleled in civil surgical experience.
The Australian Workman (Sydney) has
been taken over by the Daily Newspaper Com-
pany —it is said a syndicate of ten-pounders—who
will run it on co-operative lines, and are now
advertising shares for sale. The N.S.W. Labour-
party will never have a fa : r show until they get a
daily newspaper of their own, and in any case a
newspaper will do them more harm than good,
unless it be cleverly run and not starved in the
matter of funds.
A murderer named Schneider, at Vienna,
is in trouble for killing sixteen girls, all of
them named Maud. He seems to have had a
down on Maud, in a general way, and whenever
he met any girl of that name he slaughtered her,
and buried her mangled remains in a fore t.
What Maud had done to him is not stated, but
judging from the style in which he has been deal-
ing with her it would seem to be something pretty
bad. Schneider’s wife also helped him in pros-
pecting for Maud, but when the horrid remains
of that numerous and mangled maiden were dis-
covered, she suicided.
Things of this sort, heralding the final
crash, are now happening in Sydney every day.
A youiig woman goes into a “private bank ’ of
deposit, which owns much brass-plate and a high-
sounding name, and, producing a deposit-receipt
for £2OO, says to the cashier: “Please give me
the £2OO and interest.” Cashier looks at the
receipt ruefully, and replies with a certain amount
of pity in his tones ; “ I am very sorry to say that
I can’t do it.” “ Can’t do it!” exclaims the young
woman—“can’t do it! Then I must go to a
lawyer and make’you do it.” “ Look here,” says
the cashier, “I am not interested in this business
—I am merely a servant of the office. Take my
advice, accept £lOO now and re deposit the other
£lOO and interest for four mouths.” Finally she
acts upon his advice. Should she ever recover
her balance, it will be at the expense of the
savings of some other poor fool, deposited for the
purpose of getting high interest on no real security.
All this kind of thing reminds one of Mr.
“ Banner-Oakley,” of the Glasgow “Co-operative
Credit” Bank (interest, 18 per cent, per annum,
paid monthly), who was, some years ago, seized on
the wharf by the police as he was leaving with the
whole of the bank-assets in his carpet-bag.
Banner-Oakley got his deposits chiefly through
the persuasive eloquence of a hired lecturer. No
Sydney banker (Verb, sap.) has, up to date, been
enterprising enough to hire a lecturer. During
the triumphal progress round Scotland of Banner
Oakley’s stipendiary orator an old Scotchman one
night arose with the question : “ And, pray, how
does the banker propose to make over 18 per cent,
on his money so as to remunerate himself
for his trouble and re-pay the depositors, too?”
“ Ah,” replied the gifted lecturer, “Ican’t explain
that, but you may safely trust the matter to Mr.
Richard Banner-Oakley.”
Dear Bulletin, — I feel very tired when I
see in the Sydney morning papers every day that
Mr. Ednie-Brown, N.S.W. Director of Forests,
has proceeded to, or is about to proceed to, Hang-
man’s Elat to plant a tree, or to inspect some-
thing ; or that Mr. Ednie-Brown has been inspect-
ing the State forest at Mount Possum ; or that
Mr. J. Ednie-Brown has ordered that no more
trees be felled at Dingbat Gully. Is Mr. J.
Ednie-Brown doing all this for fun, or is a
grateful country paying him a decent screw
for it ? If so why is it necessary that we
should be made ill at breakfast-time every morn-
ing by reading that Mr. J. Ednie-Brown is doing
what he is well paid for doing? Why not let ns
all have a show : “ The energetic Mr. Muckrake,
attached to the Sydney Corporation, cleansed the
gutter in Bung-street early yesterday afternoon.
To-morrow he meets Mr. Napper by appointment
to break a heap of rocks.” And so on.— Billycan.
(There is a lot of difference between the cases of Mr
Ednie-Brown and Mr. Napper. Mr. Ednie-Brown is
somebody and is well paid, and Mr. Napper is nobody and
isn’t well paid, and it doesn’t do for a newspaper to notice
ill-paid nobodies. When State Railway-Commissioner
Eddy says lie is sick he is allowed by Government and the
newspapers a trip to Europe on full pay—£3ooo a year—
whereas when his subordinate, John Smith, the State
engine-greaser, falls sick he gets no pay and no leave—
except to go to the devil, so far as the Government
cares. State socialism is only recognised as regards the
top branches of the public services. - Ed. B.]

“ Sunset ” is a pretty little comedietta
constructed on similar lines to the author’s
“Barbara,” in that the story is chiefly un-
folded by the chatter of two girls, and that
the motive of the play is the sacrifice made
by the elder on behalf of the younger. The
renunciation in the case of Lois is that of a
prior claim to a fickle lover, while Barbara’s lay in
a failure to claim the name rightfully hers and the
property accompanying it. There is some real
jam in “ Sunset ” for those not past taking
interest in love-making. Some of Lois’ lines
are calculated to awaken tender reminiscences
in even the partially-petrified, and when
Joan, nicely played by Brenda Gibson, says,
in reference to hugging, that she “shouldn’t care
for a fellow who only talked about doing it,”
quite a sympathetic shudder went round among
the lightly-clad damsels of the dress-circle, while
the rustle of feminine apparel that floated up from
the stalls showed that the downstairs damsels
were similarly effected. filn Lois of Mary
Kingsley was very delicately handled, and her
pathos was very much truer than could have been
expected from the steely hardness of prior per-
formances. Arthur Greenaway played the seedy
young mash, whoso gloves have been cleaned and
whose boots haven’t, well, but somewhat too
earnestly. J. Cosgrove’s bumpkin lover,
Azariah Stodd, was a capital performance for so
young an actor, and “our Mary Ann” and
Harold Bussell were the stopgaps and lay
figures. Jerome’s house-warmer received a big
call on curtain-fall.
* * *
“ A Scrap of Paper ” is doubtless a lame
and impotent adaptation of Sardou’s play. It has
previously been played in Sydney by Wybert
Reeve, Genevieve Ward, the Majeronis and
Jeffreys-Lewis, and, so far as we can remember,
was no more successful on any of these occasions
than now. In the first place, it is not evident
that this particular scrap can in any way
benefit Colonel Blake, especially when, accord-
ing to his now-wedded sweetheart’s show-
ing, he must have received from her dozens
of similarly-worded communications, nor is it
apparent why, under these circumstances, the
lady should so regret its receipt by the presumably
honorable gentleman for whom it was originally
intended. Nor is the travelling gentleman’s
acumen made apparent, as the author seems to
think it is, in the place o: concealment chosen,
any more than is that of the prying lady who
discovers it with her “antennae.” Had he put
it away with his soiled linen, however, or between
the lining and sole of his slipper, or in his purse, or
worn it gummed to his chest as a porous-plaster,
of course, the play couldn’t have eventuated, and
so the author or adapter makes concealment
stupid and discovery easy. In a touch and go
light comedy character Charles Cartwright would
be excellent were he not so terribly noisy. His
defence of the civilisation of the Japs and Chows
might have been, as Bruce Smith phrases it,
“delivered from a tar-barrel in Sussex-street.”
Olga Nethersole, as Susan Hartley, has a part
that harmonises with her charming little affecta-
tions and mannerisms, while not calling on her for
any soul-harrowing “weeps,” therefore is she
thoroughly acceptable. Lawrence Cautley, hiding
his too fatal loveliness behind a tremendous beard
and yellow gaiters, was scarcely suited as a
sporting and uxorious husband, and Harold
Russell’s Doctor Penguin was chiefly remarkable
for a wonderfully good make-up. J. Cosgrove
was a particularly clumsy school-boy and other
small parts, mere “ feeders,” were ably handled by
Nellie Lyons, Mary Kingsley, Jenny Watt-
TantgT, Miss Melville, Constance Edwards, and
W. J. Beresford. The traveller’s room of the
second act disclosed a fine collection of skins,
shells, shields, spears, stuffed birds and beasts,
snakes, skulls, horns, and hides, the property of a
local collector, and Mr. Wilson is to be congratu-
lated on his conservatory scene of the last act.
»# * *
Manager Macdonald is still reluctantly
turning away money from Sydney Her Majesty’s,
and “The Lights o’ London” apparently would
run payably for at least a month more than
prior engagements will allow. This is not sur-
prising, for it is very well-staged and carefully
handled. In the workhouse, frontcloth-scene, for
instance, every entering figure is a faithful picture
of some type, and the realistic manner in which
the wind whistles through George Rignold’s
moustache is enough to make the fattest man in
the warmest corner of the house shiver till his
bones rattle. Another piece of high-art realism
which appeals to every Sydneyites’ breast is the
manner in which the London policeman is induced
to wink at the exigencies of a “jug ” business in a
late locality, and for real sensationalism give us
George Rex’s leap into the icy waters, a deed of
daring only surpassed by his subsequent over-
throw of ten policemen. By the way, if some of
these blue-coated menials of parochial despotism
(are ye listenin’, E. W. O’Sullivan ?) are not well
padded they’ll get hurt some frosty evening.
Clint and Co.’s Regent Park scene with its
background of brightly-lit streets, middle-distance
bridge swiftly crossed and re-crossed by hansoms
and tree-lined canal-banks, does them credit.
The management have followed the Bernhardtian
example and omitted a scenic catalogue from

“ A Night Off” could nob have thoroughly
satisfied the “ gallery-boy with the dreadful
whistle,” for it was withdrawn on Saturday last,
the date when “Dr. Bill” re-commenced Sydney
practice for six nights only. It could scarcely be
expected that this constantly-retiring and con-
stantly-resurrected medico, who might be styled a
regular “ Bill ’’-sticker, would at once take up
the parable where it was dropped, and so busi-
ness, at the time of writing, has been scarcely up
to the standard required of a small theatre witli
a high salary list. Pattie Browne’s kneecap is
much better, thank you, and so she is back in her
old part of the high-kicking Pauntleroy. The
place of Brenda Gibson, engaged in playing at
the opposition house, is filled by Nannio Gallagher,
formerly on the list at the other place, and R se
Brandram plays Ellen. Otherwise, parts are dis-
tributed as before, and Hamilton Aide’s adapta-
tion of Albert Carre’s play will ably hold the fort
until Saturday night, when “Devotion,” now
running in Melbourne and the first of Dot Bouci-
cault’s plays to be staged in Sydney, will be
produced.

Old Elton has been playing William Guard
during the week at Sydney Royal to somewhat
diminished houses, though flagging interest was
temporarily revived by the re-appearance ofCharles
Ryley, who now sings with much apparent effort,
as Marcell. Julia Loel has also taken a rest and
given up Fraisette to Ida Osborne, who has not
been seen or heard in Sydney for an age. Every-
thing otherwise is lovely, and the ballet is a joy
for ever, whose loveliness increases, but, never-
theless, sinks into nothingness beside that of
Elton’s self-painted portrait as “ le hrav grande
militaire,” now shown in Ascherberg’s window
free of charge. It is said that “lolanthe” is
ready to jump into the breach at an hour’s notice
should biz. run down too low. The Opera Co.
.open at Adelaide Royal in “ The Gondoliers ” on
19th September.

“ Moths ” was staged on Monday night
last at Sydney Garrick for four nights. Those
who didn’t see the prior production at this house
may find pleasure in the present one ; those who
did see it will probably view this, the latest imper-
sonation of the Ouidaesque, as an unqualified pro-
vocative to rank blasphemy. Despite a sensible
curtailment of some of the Bulwerish flapdoodle in
which “ Moths ” abounds, long- “ waits ” spun out
the show till 11 o’clock on first night, and
the whole thing didn’t seem to hit any-
one very hard. Only three of the parts, those
played by Olga Nethersole, Laurence Cautley and
Charles Cartwright, are filled as they were pre-
viously. The little lady’s Vere, despite atr inten-
sified leaning towards gurgle and gasp, is marked
by all its old earnestness and suggestion of inno-
cence and purity. Cartwright has altered the
make-up of his Zouroff, and for the worse, other-
wise the performance is up to its former high
standard of excellence. Cautley’s tenor singer
Correze has also been changed in appearance.
Now, his locks are grey, and plastered down
exactly like the curly candle-wicks of Kyrle
Bellew, and the soulful gusher drags his
toes across the stage and wheels round on one
heel with his back to the audience after the
feather-legged-rooster, Irving - Bellew - Lyndal
fashion. Sass, as Lord Jura, is sorely missed.
Harold Kingsley is no juror or we’re no judge;
nevertheless, his overthrow of Zouroff, at Szarisla,
■was excellently managed. But the supreme
•blunder of all was lifting Nellie Lyons out of
Fuchsia Leach, a part that might have been built
.round her, and fitting it on Mary Kingsley, whom
lit touches nowhere, while the former’s Lady Dolly
iis just one whit less villainous. Jenny Wati-
Tanner looks too comfortably rotund and good-
matured for the vixenish Sonnaz, and “Con-
stance Edwards is just as feeble as any other
■ordinary amateur would be as Nadine. W. J.
Wilson’s Deauville garden-scene is very fine in-
deed, and the staging throughout is up to the
present high level prevailing in Sydney. But the
weary travellers who come in from the wintry
Russian steppes, looking warm and contented,
arrayed in spotless boots and bobtailed jackets,
don’t study the harmony of things. F. C.
Phillips’and Sydney Grundy’s “ Ambassador,” a
comedy-drama, goes up on Saturday night.

The talented Dan Barry, with his company
and intelligent bull-dog, are now in Sydney. Dan
and the company and the pup intend taking a
lease of a Melbourne theatre in about three
months, when they are thoroughly rested.
** * *
The Simonsen Opera Company are sailing
over the high C’s at Melbourne Alexandra on an
even keel, and the man on the look-out —viz.,
little Martin S. —sees no ominous signs of breakers
ahead. Business was satisfactory during four
nights of “tin Ballo,” a performance which
brought prima-donna Guidotti into increased
respect as an actress, whilst “Lucia,” produced
on Saturday night, proved the success of the
season thus far. That long-sustained struggle
'between singers and orchestra, at the end of the
second act, in which the big drum plays such an
appalling part, was received with a display of
■enthusiasm that caused the company to forget
their dramatic differences and shake hands all
'round, Edgardo, indeed, put his mental distress
im his pocket and smilingly insisted that the con-
ductor should take a call, whereupon the
‘dashing Maffezzoli turned round and pointed
with his baton to the region of his heart
® s the shilling pittites waved their hats.
■Somebody even made an attempt to strike
yp “He’s a jolly good fellow” in Italian,
but lus knowledge of the language broke down at
“jolly,” so the well-meant enterprise had to be
hastily abandoned. When the excitement sub-
sided the tenor and baritone relapsed into their
family feud and commenced to clash swords, and
later in the evening the bonnie Lucia went mad as
usual, but whatever happened always appeared to
be for the best, and all the leading characters
shook hands in the most cordial manner after a
few of them were dead. “ Lucia ” was probably
as great a triumph as can be attained in Italian
opera at popular prices. The Cutticas, husband
and wife, Sisco and lorio, shared honours with
the conductor and his forces in the lump. This
(Thursday) evening “Un Ballo” is given for the
last time, “Lucia” again on Friday, and “ Les
Huguenots ” on Saturday.

Elsewhere old bills have been renewed for
the present week. At the Royal, last Friday,
they revived “ Woodbarrow Farm ” for one night
only, because it is a sort of agricultural show in its
little way, and was considered to smack of green
pastures and “well-bred cattle, marching slow.”
The course of “A Celebrated Case” was con-
tinued on Saturday, and seemed, perhaps, a trifle
fresher for its night off. This drama, as already
mentioned, is crowded with opportunities for
strong acting. Nobody can bo said to rise to any
one of several splendid situations, although Miss
Villiers puts some skilful acrobatic business into
her death-struggle in the first act. Of the others,
Miss Floyd is the most absolutely helpless when
the pinch comes, whilst little Violet Ashton (or
Baby Nichols) sets a good example to xhe whole
crowd. Boothmau, who represents the local
element in Miss Villiers’company, seems to have
been thrown in for the purpose of challenging
comparison between English tweedledum and
Australian tvveedle-dee. Boothman, however,
proves nothing except that two blacks don’t
make a white, and if it would soothe the manage-
ment to hear that this artist is an additional source
of sadness to the looker on, why the management
can accept the statement as read. “ Lost in
London ” will be put up on Saturday (sth).
