the Bijou, notwithstanding the uncanny cut of
several pairs of silk pantaloons and a superabund-
ance of false hair. Playgoers, though, are about
full up of the Richelieu period, and tired of hear-
ing that His Majesty the King is in the next
room ; moreover, respect for court suits and all
royal institutions is on the rapid decline. Bouoi-
caults little play is a clever, showy, stagey
production which never appeals to the heart, or
persuades one that it is dealing in facts ; conse-
quently, Melbourne will

Ellen ” is considered
funnier, at time of writ-

it. Ere these beautiful
lines appear in print the
plot may have changed
again for the better, and
many of the songs are
certain to be different,
because the same songs
would pall dreadfully
upon a johnnieifhehad to
hear, ’em six nights in suc-
cession and wasn’t too
drunk tounderstand. The
one really novel feature
of “ Cinder - Ellen ” is
Nellie Barren as a girl. She takes very kindly to
petticoats after all these long years of performing
in the other things, and appears to have made a
fresh start in life at the age of fifteen :
A song that Nellie sings in the schoolroom scene
is droll enough for anything. Fred. Leslie keeps
his end of the business up all the time, but pretty
Grace Pedley, Danby, Fred. Storey, and the
dancing girls are somewhat merged in a multitude
of chorus. The three fair amazons strike atti-
tudes in the front row as of yore, but they do
nothing else worth mentioning, and are generally
regarded as monotonous. “ Cinder Ellen ”
abounds in strokes of Leslie’s mechanical humour
and suggests, even more than “Ruy Bias,” that
he could arrange a harlequinade better than any
comedian on the stage. But it is to be hoped he
will not do anything so dreadful. The Gaiety
season will be followed on September 12 by “ The
Late Lamented,” a farcical comedy, in which
Snazelle makes his first appearance at the Princess’.

At the Athenreum critical exception is
taken to some of the figures in Snazelle’s views,
and people complain that the Village Blacksmith
is a different outrage upon humanity in each pic-
ture. But the recitations, songs, and yarns get
there all the same, and a vice-regal command on
the subject has been issued, A view of Hopetoun
commanding Snazelle would look very nice on the
sheet.

The Gaiety Hall was packed tight on
Saturday, and the Waxworks had a great time
during the Agricultural Show. In the course of
scouring the market for novelties Phil. Stuart has
secui'ed, at enormous cost, a pair of prize twin
babies, who may be seen drawing sustenance from
Nature’s font, to the music of a brass band.

Alfred Dampier’s Co. is doing good gallery
business at Adelaide Royal. “Shamus O’Brien,”
“IP's Natural Life,” “Miner’s Right,” and other
dramas of this description have followed in rapid
succession. The members of the company have
shown excellent results with the material at hand.
Janet Achurch, Charles Charrington and Herbert
Flemming put in four successful nights at the
Albert Hall to fill up time before departure for
West Australia. One evening was devoted to the
production for the first time in this country of
Henrik Ibsen’s “Hedda Gabler,” a weird, mys-
terious piece thoroughly characteristic of the
author. Tanner fasts away and Tommy Hudson
has returned to his old love, Garner’s theatre.
Dear Nellie, some ten thousand nights
You reigned supreme in silken tights
A thing of beauty and a joy
Forever seemed you—as a boy.
But now, in wealth of golden curls,
You figure as a queen of girls;
Old Time he winks his other eye
When Cinder Ellen capers by,
And with a wheezy laugh asserts—
“ Begad ! she’s born again—in skirts.”
At Poverty Point.
One of the ladies of the Laura Villiers company gives
herself absurd “airs” behind the scenes.
Johnel Sullivan insists upon being served with a hot
roast of meat at his Melbourne hostelry, after the drammer
is over. On at least one occasion he has started proceed-
ings by sweeping his repast off the supper-table with his
strong right arm. Probably the Slugger considered that
the times were out of joint.
Lucia Harwood recently gave a mixed matinee enter-
tainment at the Steinway Hall, London. Lucia seems to
have produced a brother who shared a duologue with her,
and a little sister Dorothy who gave clever recitations.
Robert Reece, the burlesque writer, is dead. His work
was better than Farnie’s, about as good as Burnand’s, and
many degrees below H. J. Byron’s.
An advertisement biography of a damsel calling herself
the Queen of American Dialect Readers states that “ she
eventually'enteredaconvent, where she speedilydeveloped
signs of unusual dramatic ability.” Which is more re-
markable than the instance of an Australian bard whose
genius burst into bloom in an ironmongery 7 establishment.
One of the most impromptu performances on record
was given in a certain Australasian city the other week.
The managers of a travelling show had abandoned a
sterile season in despair, and were mingling groans over
the decline of the drama when their weary eyes fell upon
a small crowd of patrons waiting outside the doors of the
deserted “house.” No management likes to turn away
good money, more especially from bare benches, so these
managers rushed round to the back, lighted the gas, and
re-opened the show with a new and special attraction for
one night only. Within half-an-hour they had rallied
their company round the standard, and started a brilliant
rendering of “ Our Boys ”on go-as-you-please lines. The
performance was a splendid financial success. One act of
Byron’s comedy sufficed to send the audience away en
viasse, after which the company cheerfully divided the
takings and withdrew likewise. It subsequently trans-
pired that two of the leading characters thought they
were taking parts in “East Lynne,” and the audience
held an indignation meeting round the corner to protest
against any further representation of “Hamlet” without
the ghost.
Laura Villiers, who complains that she has come all
these thousands of miles “ to be made feel so wretched,”
is apt to forget how many other people have been made
wretched likewise.

A few years ago, an Italian gentleman was running a
company in Queensland, and had good luck all down the
coast till he reached Brisbane. There he struck a great
financial snag. There were about four people in the
circle, and two in the stalls, and they made up a very un-
appreciative half-dozen ; also, there were some forty soul-
less clods in the pit. The Signor stood this for three
nights, and then he struck ; in other words, he got tight
and made an oration. “ Laidees an’ shenteelmen, I bring
my opeer-a companee from Singapora, Batavia, Cookev’-
town, Eockyhampytown to Brisban. We playee ‘La
Favorita,’ ‘II Trovatore,’ ‘La Traviata,’ anyding. You
canno’ appreciate it, you can go to what-yoii-call blazes.
Exycuse me, I canno’ speak very good Anglaishe.” Then
he bowed and retired.
When Irving studied Shylock, he had recourse to a
Jewish friend, who told him that the Keans, Phelps,
Brooke, and all the other actors, did the curse wrongly’
because a Jew will never kneel to curse, but stand, with
his hand on his brow. At night, Hebrew attorney, in
the stalls, said, “Well, I didn’t know Irving was one
of us.”

Amateur male leg-shows—in place of amateur theatricals
—are the latest thing now in the best American society,
The biggest show of this kind that has yet come off was a
“ charitable entertainment ” at the Garden Theatre, New
York, when Mr. Thomas Kelly, the premiere danseuse of
that city, and Mr. Charles Frazier, the premiere dansevs /
of Philadelphia, furore.” The poor, washed-
out New York dude was clean shaven, and the local press
grovels about his “ lovely rose silk gown festooned with
graceful garlands, and with diamonds glittering here and
there in the corsage.” His frock was cut low at the front,
showing a considerable section of his flat, unlovely trunk,
and his alabaster shoulders caused a pleasurable" excite-
ment; also his back was in fashion, and pre-
sented a fair mark for a kick, if his mentally diseased
audience had possessed vigour enough to give it. The
assemblage also approved of Mr. Frazier’s arms which
were white and delicately' rounded, and taking him all
over he was a soft and dainty creature, with a kissable
expression upon his rosebud mug. Nobody walloped this
pair or had them arrested. Nobody even had enough
mental vigour left to realise that the show wasn’t up to
much. The cultured and wealthy American of the new
generation is a faint, scented, mcrally-decayed creature,
who wails over the hardship of his lot if a fly settles on
his umbrella. He is made up mostly of uncounted gold,
indecent fads, and mixed drinks, and he isn’t, stricA/
speaking, either a man ora woman—heisonlya “cholly,
or a “chappie,” or some other kind of dude according to
the fashion of the moment. And if Washington could
see him it would make that dead patriot almost wish that
he had never had any Revolution at all.

London Echo : “ Every person who cannot
work must be cared for without degradation, and
every person who can work must be put in the
way of finding the work he can do. That is a big
order, but it spells civilisation. If Government
means anything but backing up the strong, it
ought to mean that. In the congested regions of
wealth we must find part of the remedy for the
congested regions of poverty.”
Premier Munro’s private secretary, Ebe-
nezer Thomas, is at war with the Premier s
orderly on the subject of a black bag. The secre-
tary instructed the orderly (a trooper) to carry the
Premier’s bag between the railway-station and
the department, whereupon the orderly said he
was not going to fetch and carry for anybody
after working hours. Then the Chief Commis-
sioner of Police transferred the orderly and pre-
vented a split in the Cabinet. Moral—Never
quarrel with anybody named Ebenezer.
“We must be in port,” said one of the
Gambler’s female passengers as, in her cabin, she
felt the Easby strike that doomed ship. A few
moments and she was in that port at which all
humans finally arrive. A dramatic story is told
of the man who, while struggling in the water on
the same dark and fateful night, buoyed up, as he
thought, his wife. When they were taken into
the boat he found the rescued one to be not his
wife (to whom he had been but five months
married), but another woman. On two previous
occasions we have seen such incidents recorded in
connection with wrecks, and Emile Souvestre has
evolved from the situation a powerful short story.

Alone.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Arthur Western had just been married. His
bride was changing the for-ever-after useless robe
which it is customary for women to wear on such
occasions, for her travelling dress, and he had
managed to detach himself from the crowd of
guests thronging his father-in-law’s residence, and
was congratulating himself upon having so adroitly
escaped from the torture of ceremony.
“ A good opportunity to read that letter of
Eclie’s,” he muttered, then added with a smile,
“How offended and indignant she would be, it
she knew I had a letter of hers three hours in my
pocket without attenuating to open it. bhe is a
little huffy, I suspect, at not having been apprised
of my intention till the last moment, but magnani-
mously forgives me and sends sisterly congratula-
tions on this happy event.”
Somehow the word “ happy ” sounded as if the
speaker doubted its aptness.
Soliloquising on its contents he opened the letter
he had taken from his pocket, remarking as he
glanced at its. date that it had been delayed a
couple of days by the floods. The letter ran
thus: —
My Dear Arthur,—I presume you had some good reason
for keeping me in ignorance of your matrimonial inten-
tions till they have almost been consummated, though 1
am unable to imagine w hat your reason can be.
Perhaps you guessed what my feelings would be ou
hearing that you were going to marry one woman, know-
ing as 1 do that you love another. For that you still
love Ethel Herbert I am convinced. If you marry anyone
but her I shall be more disappointed in you than 1 ever
was before in any human being. Thank Heaven this
letter will be in time to prevent you committing the act
of a madman. Weigh carefully what I have to tell you.
You remember that evening on which you came home
from Randwick races and told us that you had lost ±700,
and that as there was no possible chance of your being
able to find such a sum there was nothing for it but to
make yoursilt “scarce.” Well, I am an
inexperienced fool knowing nothing of the
ways in which men manage to tide over
such difficulties. I have conjured up
dreadful ghostlike pictures of the
brother I loved hiding from his
creditors and the police.
I felt as if I should go mad,
and at last resolved to un-
burden my mind to my one
friend and confidante, Ethel
Vincent. I told her every-
thing. Poor girl! I know
now that my agony of mind
was as nothing compared to
hers, though at the time the
selfishness of my own grief
prevented me from seeing any-
thing of hers. She walked
home with me, assuring me
that everything would come
r'ght, and that a day some-
times brought forth wonderful
things. That day, or rather night,
did. Next morning a parcel was
handed in for you by a man whom the
servant said she had never seen before. It
contained £700 in notes, together with a
tc apof paper,which merely said—
“ In case what is intended as a gift should,
as such, burden the mind of the recipient, it can, when-
ever convenient (the sender prays not bcjore ), be placed
with Messrs. Wynne and Wynne to the credit of Gustavus
Smith.”
W r e tried for hours to guess who could have sent you
such timely assistance. .
Three days afterwards Ethel Vincent was married to
that rich old Herbert whose admiration for her we had
always treated as a joke, and whose importunities had
always seemed to cause her so much worry and annoy-
ance. I knew then who had sent the £700, Ethel had
sold herself to get the money for you ! It was one of
those actions which find nothing but condemnation in the
minds of your goody-goodies with their everlasting cant
of thought and speech, but I am wicked and romantic
and stupid enough to look upon it as a grand sacrifice,
grandly made by a woman for the man whom she loved.
I went to her, and with a sorrow that was largely
alloyed by my relief at the result of her action, told her
I had guessed her secret. The manner in which she
received my communication awed me as nothing ever did
before or since. She made me solemnly promise never to
divulge what I knew. f have faithfully kept that
promise till now, though I found it hard to do so at times
when you spoke so slightingly of Ethel and her falseness
to you. ,
Six weeks ago Mr. Herbert died—m his wife s arms,
adoring her to the last, I am told. Ethel is free. She
still loves you, for such women love but one man. I can-
not believe that the love which was so strong within yen
two years ago is dead already—nay, I believe that if you
were to be married a dozen times over yon would still
love Ethel. I suppose I have done a most culpable and
dishonourable thing in thus breaking my promise, but
ob ! Arthur, I could not let you wreck your life and
Ethel’s when speaking might prevent it.
I was so upset by the announcement of your intended
marriage that I should have set out for Wurra Wurra at
once, hut, thanks to that wretched buggy accident, I am
still a cripple. However, this will reach you on the 13th ;
the contemplated marriage is fixed for the 15th. You will
have time to devise some way of getting over it. 1 am
truly sorry for your fiancee, but I am inclined to think it
will not take her a lifetime to get over her disappoint-
ment, since it is just three months since her engagement
to Mr. Cole was broken off.
Think of Ethel, Arthur ! Anxiously waiting to hear the
steps which you have taken. Your loving sister,
Eire.
There fell on the summer air u cry which racked
and tore a strong man's frame in its passage from
heart to lips.
Chapter II.
At the side of a tiny lagoon nestling at the foot
of the hill on which stood her house, Ethel Her-
bert stood one evening watching the water-liens
playing with their young. She was too absorbed
in thought to notice the horseman entering the
gate behind her. But he saw the solitary form
standing by the lake and recognised it. He dis-
mounted and went up to her.
“ Ethel, I have been struggling for months
against the wish to come and see you. I could
hold out no longer. ’
The woman so greeted looked dumbly at the
speaker for a moment, then, though her heart
throbbed painfully, said in a cold, calm voice—
“ It’s kind of you to come and see me.”
The formality of her manner made Arthur
Western shiver. For a moment he paused irre-
solute ; then, ere she could divine his intention
made a sudden step towards her and took her in
his arms as he said, “ I can no longer be deceived
by your acting. Edie has told you that I know
all. My darling, my darling, can you ever for-
give me for being such a blind, mistaken fool?”
Perhaps a woman is never so entirely subjugated
by love as when she finds that, allied to the
strength of her lover, is a daring which bewilders,
half frightens, but wholly enthralls her.
Her mask of reserve was torn off ; her armour of
cold formality dropped into a thousand pieces at
her feet; and the woman who, through months of
wretchedness, had proved herself patient in suf-
fering, strong in endurance, sobbed in her lover’s
arms like an over-wrought child. Something of
triumph mingled with the love on Arthur
Western’s face as he softly stroked her hair and
soothed her with the words that come so readily
to the lips when love is in the heart, and the
beloved in the arms of the speaker.
The storm raged itself out. Ethel at last raised
her head, and said in unsteady tones :
“ You must not stay. Please go, Arthur !”
“ Yes, yes, darling, when you have said when I
may come back. What would our lives be worth
to us, Ethel, if we had to spend them apart ?”
He had resisted her efforts to get free. His
arms were still around her. His every word
sounded like a caress.
“ What else can we do ?” she said, miserably.
He held her yet more closely as he whispered :
“Do you, then, care so much for the world’s
opinion ?”
“ No,” she answered. “ Eor th.at I care nothing
—absolutely nothing. When a woman has for-
feited her own respect she cares little for that of
other people. It would be difficult for a woman
who has been the wife of a man for whom she has
no love, to reach any greater depth of degradation.
No, it is not that which I think of, but ’’—she
paused slightly—“if there were nothing else
tb°re is your wife. Think of her suffering.”
IT WOULDN’T FOLLOW.
TRAM-CONDUCTOR : “What have you got under your coat? An, a dog, I see.”
PASSENGER: “So it is.”
TRAM-CONDUCTOR : “You’ll have to put him outside. He must follow the car.”
PASSENGER: “ No, I won’t, my dog can’t run.”

PASSENGER: “’Cause my dog can’t run.”
POLICEMAN: “ Show me the dog ! ”
PASSENGER : “There you are? I’ve just fetched him from the bird-stuffer's."
“ Her suffering!” he echoed disdainfully. “ Her
freedom is the only gift I could give her that
would evoke her gratitude. She married me out
of pique. Her lover has returned to his allegiance.
She would welcome the opportunity to fly to his
arms. So much for that scruple. Dearest, you
arc mine, mine !” he cried, with passionate force,
“ and no one shall stand between us.”
“Yes,” she said despairingly, “there is one —
there always will be one! Oh, Arthur, listen.
The man whose name I bear was good to me with
a goodness indescribable. My coldness and in-
difference, which, try as I would, I could not help
showing, must have been maddening, but his
gentle forbearance never once faltered. There
were times when another man would have felt
tempted to kill me, but he uttered ro word of
reproach. I sometimes think I broke his heart.
He treated me with the same considerate goodness
to the last —to the last he even loved me. He
died blessing me.” Her voice grew firmer: “I
care nothing for the world’s opinion, but he did.
He cherished the traditions of an old and proud
family. He held the honour of his name above
all tilings. I, on whom he lavished all the love
and generosity of a great, noble heart, will do
nothing to stain the name he held so sacred.” Her
tone changed from one of proud decision to one of
passionate pleading : “ Arthur, if indeed you care
for me, cease to tempt me and go. ”
He stood for a moment at her decree, then the
selfishness of the lover was overmastered by the
more generous feelings of the man. He bared his
Lonfl an 1 bonding reverently towards her in the
dusk, said:—
“ I, for whom you have sacrificed so much, may
well spare you the sacrifice of your loyalty to the
Dead—aye, even though to leave you kill me !”
She lay passive in his arms while ho pressed
upon her lips his kisses of farewell. Presently he
put her from him saying, hoarsely
“Co, for God’s sake, while I am able to let
you !”
She turned away without a word.
A step brought him to her side again.
“ I must help you up the hill.”
“ No,” she said, “ I will go alone.”
And all her life after it seemed to her as if, with
the same numb feeling at her heart and the same
shaking limbs, she was walking up-hill— a’ono.

The Melbourne Post-office clock joke dies
hard. Smith, M.L.A., has been asking the
P.M.C. to issue a diagram or key to the expensive
toy, on the assumption that passers-by are unable
to tell the time o’ day by a dial on which the
ordinary Roman numerals are represented by
plain electrically lighted disks. Yet the clock, as
it stands, can be read by any ordinary child, so
Smith’s alleged inability to take in the situation
at a glance will come under the head of Parlia-
mentary Intelligence, and excite no interest.

“Caesar’s Column.’*
THE BOOK OF THE HOUR.
Ignatius Donnelly has for a season ceased to
speculate on sunken continents or wandering
comets. He no longer wiles away a cultured
leisure in reconstructing a prehistoric earth ; the
famous cryptogram lias been shelved for a season,
and the big-brained reconstructive scientific critic
of the past takes a long leap into the future, and
paints, with pigments made of humanity’s heart’s-
blood, a picture of the days to be. He does not,
like Bellamy, give us a social paradise of dead-
level industrialism, nor, like William Morris, a
retrogressive heaven of communistic happiness.
Writing under the name of Edmund Boisgilbert,
M.D., Donnelly attempts to give the results of
what he considers to be, a hundred years hence, a
logical development on present lines of nineteenth
century material civilisation.
* * #
“ Caesar’s Column ”is never feeble. It is often
fiercely powerful. In some passages it throbs
with a lyrical eloquence, in others it is passionately
denunciatory; but it never forgets its science or
its fidelity to the Darwinian theory of necessity
and development.
* # #
“ Christ was only possible in a bare-footed
world ; and the few who wore shoes murdered
him,” This is the key-note of Donnelly’s book.
Faith and civilisation are incompatible. New wo
iiave a choice. Now we may stop and take stock.
In a hundred years it will be too late! Given
existing conditions unchecked, and civilisation
must commit suicide. The book is luridly
fascinating. New York, the scene of the story, is
an earthly heaven, where man (or rather,
capitalism) has brought nature under foot, and
chained science to his car as a slave. Air-ships
scour the atmosphere. Electricity threads the
earth. Money is the only god, and human
brotherhood has perished from off the face of the
earth. One-seventh of the entire population riot
in soulless luxury. The great bulk of the people
lead and live the lives of brutes lower than the
beasts that perish. Then comes the inevitable
crash. Civilisation in the twentieth century
totters over the brink of Tophet, and falls into aii
abyss of chaotic Sheol, in which all art, science,
beauty and loveliness perish together. The
French Revolution is a child’s dream compared to
the horrors conjured up by the writer of “Caesar's
Column.” The outlook for humanity for
thousands of years afterwards is blacker than
midnight. To-day the ignorant mob triumphs
over the brutal plutocracy. To-morrow it will
starve. The day after it will devour its own flesh
and blood ; and civil government of the rudest
kind will take long, long centuries to reestablish
itself. Ignorance, passion, suspicion, brutality,
cruninality, will stand as 'lions in the path of
Humanity’s progress:—
Men who have such dreadful memories of labour can
scarcely be forced back into it. After about three-fourtbs
of the human family have died of hunger, or been killed,
the remainder, constituting, by the law of the survival cf
the fittest, the most powerful and brutal, wi.l find it
necessary, for self-defence against each other, to fonn
squads or gangs. The greatest lighter in each of tluse
will become chief, as among all savages. Then the history
of the world will be slowly repeated, A bold rutllan will
conquer a number of the adjacent squads, and become a
king. Gradually, and in its rudest forms, labour will begin
again; at first exercised principally by slaves. Men will
exchange liberty for protection. After a century or two
a kind of commerce may arise. Then will follow other
centuries of wars, between provinces or nations. A new
aristocracy will spring up. Culture will lift its head. A
great power, like Home in the old world, may arise. Some
vast superstition may take possession of the world; and
Alfred, \ ictoriaand Washington maybe worshipped, as
Saturn, Juno, and Hercules were in the past; with,
perhaps, dreadful and bloody rites like those of the
Carthaginians and ancient Mexicans. And so, step by
step, mankind will re-enact the great human drama,
which begins always with a tragedy, runs through a
comedy, and terminates in a calastrophe.
Thus the great wheel of time turns for ever on,
and perfected humanity remains the idle vision of
a poet’s brain. The “ Caesar’s Column ” which
gives its name to the book is a grisly monument
reared to the insatiable genius of bloody revolt,
erected by a leader of “ The Brotherhood of
Destruction,” one Caesar Lomellini, a monster
bred by the remorseless civilisation which he is
born to overthrow. His trophy is a towering
column, piercing the sky, built of the slaughtered
bodies of those who represented the order of
things annihilated, the human bricks of the
destroyed edifice of a rotten social system. These
corpses are arranged within wooden frames, layer
upon circular layer, around a central core of
dynamite, so that the man who disturbs or
pulls down the ghastly pile will wreck the earth
for his pains. As each course is finished, cement
is poured over it, and the human tower ascends
another space into the blue sky. One of the con-
cluding scenes of this bloody revolt is powerfully
sketched. From a parapet the supposed writer
view’s an angry, surging mob approaching under
the darkness of night to an attack on a barricaded
mansion. Their march is heralded by shrieks and
wolfish howls, flames and smoke shoot through
the gloom and attend their blood-stained progress,
and high aloft in the fitful glare is to be seen,
carried upon along pole, the gory, freshly-severed
head of their erstwhile leader, Csesar Lomellini,
the brutal giant who commanded “The Brother-
hood of Destruction,” and whose column will
remain as a mortuary memorial of the death and
burial of modern civilisation.
At the recent Eton College cadet review a
shot was fired “by accident.” The German Em-
peror, however, alleges that he distinctly heard a
bullet whizz past him.
It is claimed, by a good authority, that out
of every 100 society men who gamble heavily three
cheat habitually, and out of every hundred women,
six don’t play a square game.
A clear head is indicative of good health and
regular habits. When the body is languid, and
the mind works sluggishly, Ayer’s Cathartic Pills
will assist in the recovery of physical buoyancy
and mental vigor.

tury, by Edmund Boisgilbert, M.D. (Ignatius Donnelly).

POLITICAL
POINTS
The fourth party in the N.S. W. Assembly
—the tea-party ; the small tea-party.
It is nearly time the N.S. W, Government
parrot was taught some other word besides
“ ’ject!”
Premier Fysh, of Tasmania, now frankly
admits that Parkes’ visit to Tasmania was “ a
great disappointment.”
The Victorian Government decides to
shunt out all the police-officers and men over GO.
This involves £lO,OOO a year pensions.
G. H. Reid says that if N.S W. takes the
right to vote from those who live on charity, some
legislators will be awkwardly placed.
The, easy victory in the Victorian Legisla-
tive Assembly against plural-voting was chiefly
due to the fatal advocacy of the Argus.
A sign of the times is that Dealt in has
declared that “ right, time, and the majority ” ar e
in favour of the Victorian liquor-prohibitionists.
Under the S.A. First Offenders Act (now
throe years old), 20 phi-oners have been released
without punishment. Of these but two have been
rearrested.
The N.S.W. Civil Service Commission
recommended wholesale reforms in the Postal and
Telegraph Departments. Result, the dismissal of
one temporary officer !
Graham Berry has at last to “turn up’
the Victorian Agent-Generalship—-in favour of
Gillies. The Age of yore styled Berry “ the Run-
down Dingo”—and now he will return with his
tail between his legs.
S A. Women’s Franchise Bill has been
carried in the Conservative Legislative Council by
a good majority, only three voting against it.
Here is the milk in the cocanut, however. There
is a property qualification, and consequently all the
Tory legislators rushed the measure, posing for
the nonce as democrats.
G. R. Dibhs and Bruce Smith may often
he observed coquetting behind the “bar”—when
Parkes is not about. Parkes is in bad health
physically and politically, and both Dibbs and
Bruce Smith love to exercise power. A Smith-
Bibbs Cabinet is quite feasible in the present low
state of the political barometer.
Dear Bulletin, —Frank Cotton, M.P.,
sententiously says the rejection of Parkes’ “ward ”
system by the N.S.W. Assembly is one of those
votes no one can understand. Mr. Cotton, of
course, voted with Parkes and against the Labour
Party. The question is—Should not Mr. Cotton
be directed to his natural seat, right behind
Parkes’ coat-tails ?—E. A. R.
The national and unsectarian education
policy of South Australia is being tampered with
by the Legislative Council. A proposal for a
capitation grant of £1 per head has been tacked
on to the Free Education Bill. The Assembly,
however, is almost certain to reject the new
clauses. If this happens, it is quite probable the
Council will throw out the Bill.
At the last general election a majority of
the _S. A. legislators promised to support the
remission of the Customs duties on tea, sugar,
cocoa, coffee, and kerosene, so as to reduce the
taxation on the poorer classes. Now that the
matter is before the House, many of them are
looking about for excuses to back out on, so as to
guard the land from paying more.
An effort is being made by the W.A.
Government to induce emigration from the Crofter
Isles to that province. A trial shipment of 50
families has been arranged, and should these bo
satisfied with their prospects, a large influx of
population from the Isle of Lewis is expected. The
Government are prepared to lease farms to the
emigrants on exceptionally favourable terms.
Apropos of the Australian cruisers. W hen
Admiral Aslanbegoff, of the Russian cruisers, in-
vited Melbourne citizens to a trip round Hobson’s
bay, in his mkish Afrika, they could not help
noticing, on the cuddy table, Semmes’ “Recol-
lections of his Alabama Voyages,” Every Russian
war-vessel was supplied with a copy. But English
commerce will, in t : me of war, all fly to the
American flag.

The Canadian census shows that Canada
, las > within the last ten years, lost a million people
W emigration to the United States. Too much
mponal grovel and too much of ‘‘ Old To-morrow ”
the things that are wrong with the Dominion.
The population of Canada apparently consists
now of 1,000,000 escaped American cashiers, and
about 3,000,000 dishonest Government contractors,
and there is no good in any of them.
The new edition of the N.S.W. Parlia-
Handbook, though a credit to the
printer, is no credit to the compilers. It is remark-
able for what it does not contain. In the hands
of an outsider it might have been made a valuable
work of reference. For example, why should it
not contain the standing rules qf. the. British
House of Commons, which are adopted by the first
standing* order of the N.S.W. Assembly?
Sir Robert Hamilton’s time as Governor
of Tasmania is to be extended 12 months or so to
fit into his pension date. Which, of course,
means he will not go out on foreign service again.
I fie English Government had to ship him off from
Ireland in consequence of his sympathy for
Ireland and the Irish, and because he was far too
popular there, and will probably refrain from
appointing him there again, for the same reason.
Napoleon I. is said —probably it isn’t true
—to have once published an edict, which ran
thus : “ It is hereby ordered that all misery shall
cease within 14 days.” Anyhow, that is just the
way the average liberal monarch or enlightened
aristocrat does start to put down misery. And
if anyone is found miserable after the expiration
of 14 days, he wants to hang that individual for
breaking the statute. William of Germany is a
reformer of just this pattern.

Apropos the N.S.W. Assembly electors’
rights debate. When “ Dick ” Ireland, Q.C., in-
troduced the principle of shilling electors’ rights a
large number of single men were on the West
Melbourne roll, and were much troubled how to
preserve the “right” until needed. In this
emergency a local publican offered the use of his
cash-box, and in it some three or four hundred
“rights” lay snugly by until needed at a general
scrimmage. The whole of the owners seldom
turned up at an election, but the “rights’’were
used all the same.
Parkes’ Electoral Bill is being radically
altered in committee. Already it has been
amended in three “ vital” parts ; and still Parkes
hangs on to his portfolio. He pockets the insults
—for the present, anyhow. One night he spread
a report that if the House did not stand by his
“ ward ” system, he would throw the Bill “under
the table.” The Labour-party rejected his stolen
fad, and he took care not to be in the House when
the division was taken. A message was sent to
Parkes that, if he did not sit up straight and drop
his bluff, he might be thrown “ under the table ”
in advance of his Bill.
Dear Bulletin, —If, as a correspondent
states, there be one traitor in the N.S.W. Labour
Party who carries caucus news to the Ministerial-
ists, then there is at least’another who gives
similar intelligence to the Opposition, for they
get just as early information of what goes on at
the caucus meetings as do the other side. I don’t
think, however, that “traitor” is the term —
“ blabster ” would, perhaps, be more appropriate.
I imagine these breaches of confidence are due to
the leakage, or overflow, of frail or diminutive
vessels, incapable of retaining the great in quan-
tity, or the strong in quality—irresponsible beings
from whom secrets ooze as easily as perspiration in
warm weather.—Yours, Okoss-Benoheb.
Says the Duke of Argyll in a prolonged,
apology for his existence :
Capital is the storages of labour and is purely the work
of mind in the past and its great weapon in the future.
As usual, the question is here begged by the
assumption that “mind,” if not the exclusive
possession of monopoly, is, under all conditions,
exercised by its possessors solely on behalf of the
monopolist. As if men could not, and would not,
display as much forethought, economy, and
energy in the storage of past labour and its
employment in reproductive effort on behalf of
the community as they do now on behalf of an
employer who dearly sells them the privilege to
use their talents for his benefit.
The Electoral Bill is being dangled before
the N.S.W. Parliament in order that Parkes may
distract attention from the fiscal issue. At its
present rate of progress the Bill will not be ready
for the Council in many weeks ; the Seats Re-
distribution Bill will then provide six weeks’ work
for the Assembly; and while the latter Bill is
being revised in the Council Parkes will demand
his Estimates iciththe fiscal issue still sunk, and, if
need be, defy the Labour Party to raise that
awkward issue. The Estimates must be given as
the price of the Electoral Reform, or else Parkes
will ride for a fall, and those measures will go by
the board, one being useless without the other.
“Concessions in return for support” will be
Parkes’ trump card in December.
The Constitution of N.S.W. says a holder
of a Government billet is not eligible to be a
member of Parliament. Mr. Jacob Garrard held
‘ an office of profit under the Crown ” for some
months prior to his recent election. A Royal
Commissioner may draw tw'o guineas a sitting for
taking part in a political farce such as Parkes’
costly and fruitless Civil Service Commission ; in
the following week he may run for a vacant seat
in the Assembly ; and, if defeated in the contest,
he may resume his grip on the people's guineas on
his mere motion. The question is, whether Mr.
Garrard should not absolutely have resigned the
chairmanship of the Civil Service Commission—
not merely resigning his grip on the guineas for
the time—before he was nominated for Central
Cumberland ?
Here is a fair example of the irony of fate.
When Burgess was in office in Tasmania he fixed
up a large and healthy deficiency, and left it to
his successor to liquidate. The successor toiled
like a weary Trojan for four years, and nearly
broke his back and his heart over the job, but he
did it at last. He got up the first surplus that his
poor, tired little island had seen for a long, long
spell. He was proud of his surplus, and used to
sit up at night and chuckle over it; and then he
deposited it in Burgess’s bank, and the bank broke
and now he can’t get it out. He got Burgess’s
deficiency, and now Burgess has got his credit
balance; and on top of it all he lias got to raise a
lot more money, and lend it to the bank, in tin?
hope of smoothing things till he gets the first lot
back again. Truly this is a tough globe to live in.
It is looking more and more likely every
day that Parkes will fool the N.S.W. Labour-
party badly with his Representation of the People
Bill. No one can reasonably suppose that the old
man wants to abolish plural voting, and thus
break up the strength of the importer and the
Foreign-trade landlord, but he wants badly to
keep the Labour vote as long as possible. There-
fore, his Bill is so big and cumbersome that it may
not be half finished at the end of the session, and
all that time Parkes will be a tired martyr per-
spiring at the pump-handle in the cause of
democracy. He will look, in short, six times more
of a Labour member than the whole Labour-party
put together—but the results will be nil, owing to
unforeseen circumstances over which Parkes does
not want to have any control. At present the
wily Premier is making as much toil and row as
if he were a dray and four horses engaged in
humping one reel of cotton over a level road, and
when he falls down dead in the shafts it will be
the weight of that reel of cotton that did it.
G. D. Clark, M.L.A., lately “drew atten-
tion ” in N.S.W. Assembly to the fact that the
postmistress at Leichhardt (Sydney) has a husband
in the N.S.W. Railway department—the in-
tended inference being no doubt that that lady
should be sacked on the “one man one billet”
theory. G. D. Clark himself intends to stick to
his job as (stipendiary) editor of a teetotal publi-
cation as well as to his (stipendiary) legislative
profession, on the ground that he “doesn’t know
how long the latter affair may last. ” The Bulletin
doesn’t know either. Also, as man is mortal, the
Leichhardt postmistress doesn’t know how long her
husband may last. Moreover, if a woman is to
have a vote why shouldn’t she also have a situa-
tion? And where does the “one man one billet ”
come in if Clark is to have two billets, and two
other people are only to be allowed one between
them ? This seems like Clark having four times
as many billets as anybody else. The situation
seems to be something like this : (1) One man,
one job; (2) All men shall be free and equal;
(3) Borne men shall be a lot more equal than
others; (4) G. D. Clark shall be these men.
A SYDNEY JEWELLER’S.
In Pitt-street, at the corner of the new Imperial
Arcade, a jeweller’s shop has lately been opened,
the arrangement of which is such as to attract
attention and admiration. Messrs. Dahms &
Company, late of the King-street Arcade, are the
proprietors, and the new premises are creditable
alike to their enterprise and artistic taste. A
jeweller’s business, perhaps more than all others,
is one demanding the exercise of tasteful judg-
ment, because the staple commodity is necessarily
an artistic one, jewellery being, in most cases, more
an article of ornament than use. Good taste—the
capacity for determining that which most closely
approximates to the truly beautiful—is essential
to a good jeweller, for it is only by the
exercise of this quality that he can hope to
anticipate and minister to the aesthetic demands
of the public. To be able to successfully antici-
pate public demand in this respect, requires more
than ordinary judgment and perception, for it is
essential that there should be also a knowledge of
the direction in which the popular aesthetic faculty
is developing. Thus it is, then, that when looking
at the articles of jewellery so attractively dis-
played by Messrs. Dahms and Co., one under-
stands the secret of the rapid growth of this firm
from a position of comparative obscurity to that
of one of the leading jewellers—brooches, bracelets,
rings, pins, chains, bangles, earrings, all bearing
in the midst of their profusion the unmistakeable
evidence of cultivated taste and commercial
ability. Inside the shop, the handsome show-
cases and fittings, made of polished walnut, and
specially designed to suit the requirements of
their business, indicate the completeness of a well-
ordered business. On the shelves of the cases a
glittering display of electro-plate may be seen,
relieved here and there by the brighter tints of
coloured cut-glass bowls and dishes and the softer
hues of pearl-glass and Crown-Derby ware. In
the electro-plate window these goods repose on
rich-coloured velvet and plush, the tints of which
are evidently chosen to present an effective and
pleasing contrast. Table-casters, fruit-dishes,
berry-bowls, flower-stands, spirit-frames, ice-
pitchers, cups and goblets vie with each other in
soliciting admiration. In another window,
arranged for the display of marble-clocks, an
equally artistic picture is presented. Prettily-
draped shelves show intending purchasers how
the different clocks would look on their mantle-
pieces at home, and so enables them to
make a selection under advantageous circum-
stances. In the jewellery window the goods
are displayed on various tints of velvet
that their effect may be seen when worn
on the different coloured dress fabrics. Messrs.
Dahms and Company can be especially recom-
mended to intending purchasers, by reason of
the utility and beauty of their goods, from a
gold brooch or ring to a silver-mounted walking-
stick or gold match-box, and from a salt-cellar to
a tea and coffee service or punch-bowl. Their
stock is varied, extensive and well-assorted, and
the prices moderate. The partners, Mr. Dahms
and Mr. Rollason, conduct their business per-
sonally, and their object is evidently to secure in
each customer a permanent and continued patron,
and being assured of this, they will doubtless con-
tinue to be successful.


SHEARING SEASON.
The Garners and the Camels.
iSJotmKE, Thursday.
It’ is generally
understood that several large stations are
going to import camels here io order to
crush the carrying trade. The names of
Nocolecho and Elsinora stations are men-
tioned, the latter taking 150 camels. In all
it is said 600 camels will be introduced here
this season. The townspeople -will agitate
their membe/s to get A short act passed to
not alhaw camels within a certain radius of
the town, on the ground that they frighten
horses and will causa a number of acci-
dents.

Rumour goes that the authorities at Hobart
did not succeed in obtaining the services of a
professional hangman to execute Cooley for the
Richmond murder. Old Solomon Blay is no
longer up to the work, so had to resign. The fix
got so serious on the morning of the execution that
there was nothing else for it bub to disguise the
Sheriff himself in black wig, painted face, and
spectacles, and let him perform, just once in a
way, the solemn duties of his dignified office.

In announcing Captain De Moleyns’ en-
gagement to Miss Wilson, a Victorian paper
remarks that the gallant Captain “ fell under the
signal disapproval of LordHopetoun while in Vic-
toria, and consequently gave up his post and
returned to England.” People don’t know so
much about that. All they seem quite sure of
is that De Moleyns fell under the signal disap-
proval of a man out Toorak way.

this world.

When Christie Murray says that Aus-
tralians are “ drunken ” he writes bosh. George
Augustus Sala, a much closer observer, gives it as
his opinion that the Australian youth is pro-
voking! y sober. When Murray spoke of drunken
Australians he probably meant drunken English-
men in Australia. Among D. C. M.’s pals in this
country were quite a large number of imported
people, each of whom sported a whisky-volcano on
each square inch of his countenance.
* # #
Some more of the troubles of him who
teaches the young idea how to shoot. A N.S.W.
country teacher writes in a pathetic strain to tell
us how he gave a scrap-book as a prize to a small
girl in his class. Next day the recipient’s mother
sent it back with an indignant intimation that she
didn’t care for a book of blank paper, and that as
he had already cut out and appropriated half the
leaves he might keep the boards as well. Truly
it is heavy shovelling to be a back-blocks in-
structor.
# * *
It has suddenly been discovered that a
pious and God-fearing semi-limb of the law who
recently left a Sydney suburb for the Golden
Shore was not as good and godly as he ought to
have been. We remember him in the early sixties
when he was the most attentive J.P. on the Mel-
bourne roster, the constant colleague of E. P. S.
Sturt at the City Police Court, and frequently
dispensing ready justice with a lone hand. The
“ forty shillings without the option,” or a
“month’s hard,” came as glibly to his tongue as
to that of the oldest and best-paid P.M. But a
day of trouble was at hand, and the City Bench
and Sergeant Pewtress knew him no more. It
was said that a certain scientific society, of which
he was treasurer, mourned his departure. In
Sydney he surrounded his head with a halo
that the angels might envy, and now we
hear that some thousands of pounds sterling
in trust moneys, the hoardings of widows
and orphans, the savings of careful mechanics
and the “ wee bit money ” put by for a
rainy day have gone at one swell swoop. As long
as the big interest was paid regularly, and it was
paid regularly, the investors did not care to
enquire what kind of security their “ dear friend ”
had invested in. His death proved that his assets
were next to nil and his liabilities some ten thou-
sand, and of such is the Kingdom of Heaven.
However, The Bulletin is scarcely called upon to
sympathise with people who take big interest and
ask no questions.
On a Warrego sauclndsre, August 8, 1891.

* # •
The imported versus Australian marble
controversy in N.S.W. Parliament reminds one
that years ago Gavan Duffy got into trouble in
Victoria for proposing to use Italian marble in
building Parliament Houses, Melbourne, the
allegation being that “he ordered it because the
Pope had an interest in the quarries.” Yale, or
somebody like that, asked, one night, what kind
of marble had been decided on. Whereupon Dick
Ireland, in Daffy’s absence, said “he didn’t quite
know, but he had heard that it was intended to
use Roman cement,” Which quip settled the
matter.

The Contemporary Review been
gushing over the alleged fact that Albert Edward
is by no means a very bad sort of a husband. It
ought to be easy enough for an Heir-Apparent to
keep up a respectable semblance of conjugal
regard on £150,000 a year and free lodgings. The
real trouble is to idolise your wife when she can’t
get any more “tick” from the sordid-minded
grocer.

It was on the last day of the tour that the
great pianist and tuner met for the last time.
During 20 long years the wealthy piano firm had
told him off to accompany the distinguished
artist, and in all that time he had never been
asked even “if he had a mouth.” The famous
man loquitur : “Oh, Smith, I’ve a little thing I’d
like to give you before saying good-bye, if you will
call at the hotel.” The poor tuner Jog. : “ Oh, it’s
very good of you, sir,” and promised to call.
Smith spends the meantime dreaming of the many
precious things that would be very suitable
rewards after 20 years’ faithful service, and calls
at last at the hotel. Not without a certain shy-
ness, he is shown into the presence of the
maestro, who commences : “ Oh, Smith, I thought
I’d like to give you this before leaving,”- and,
putting his hand into his pocket, he continues :
“ I don’t think I shall require it any more.” It
was the key of the piano. . . . On the second
tour there was a new tuner.

But for his presence of mind a Sydney,
celebrity would have been in a tight place the
other day. He is a fat, unctuous, long-nosed old
man, with a wife and a large, grown-up family—
the sort of man who is a temporary father to the
ballet-maiden, and a counsellor to the milliner’s
little girl, and who likes to take the distressed
barmaid to his enormous bosom, and comfort her.
The last time a girl wanted comforting he diag-
nosed the case, and decided that a tete-a-Ute
dinner would best suit her complaint, so he made
an appointment with her, and ordered the meal
at a swell restaurant. It was a mighty and
expensive feed, and the old man was there to
time, and sat gloating over the victuals. He
waited, and sniffed, and waited. Then a dainty
tread was heard upon the stairs, and another
tread that wasn’t dainty came behind it. The
door opened ; it was the Jong-expected one. He
was just about to wobble into her arms, when his
wife’s visage appeared over the visitor’s shoulder.
Then the girl remarked pleasantly: “This is a
little surprise, Mrs. F.; Mr. F. has got ready for
you, so I won’t detain you any longer,” and,
shaking hands with Mrs. F., she cleared_ out.
And Mr. F. cal ml} 7 embraced his good old wife in-
stead of the girl, and told her how the dinner and
the wines were intended to celebrate the anni-
versary of the death of his aunt’s infant—it was
the only thing he could remember at the moment
—and a hilarious evening was spent. Presence
of mind is a great institution, and has pulled many
a petrified capitalist through.

While Grand Master Governor Jersey was
delivering an esoteric oration to the Barrier
Masons, he detected the vibration of a curtain at
one end of the room. It was suddenly drawn
aside, when twenty listeners, male and female,
were disclosed.

Which starts from this your jubilee,
The same pure spirit still impart
To English wit and English art.”

All the gapeseed of the Collins-street
Block is devoted to the late Dr. Beaney’s
jewellery. There it lies, cushioned, in a jeweller’s
window. The huge diamond snake-rings that
adorned his digits, and which he swept off into a
basin before operating; the eye-closers that blazed
on his cambric bosom, the wrist-sparklers.
Bathetic is the sight of the double chain which
meandered over his corporation, and into the two
waistcoat pockets.

The suspension of the Y.D.L. Bank
afforded some Hobart folks amusement in the
spectacle of a prominent resident in the prime of
life (holder of a “ fat ” Government appointment)
publicly wringing his hands and actually weeping
over his probable loss. In this condition he was
met by a lady relative whom he greeted with
“ All’s gone, ’Melia, everything’s'lost! ” to which
she un'eelingly responded, “ Go home, you fool.”

lb is said that the last conspicuous de-
faulter who got away from Adelaide left in a
clean-shaved state and disguised as a middle-aged
widow. He was dressed in deep black, and an
accomplice, who addressed him as “ mother,” saw
him on board, and placed him in charge of the
captain. The widow wept bitterly at parting,
and kissed her alleged offspring, and then tottered
feebly to the seclusion of the —ef —ladies’ cabin.

One of the leading lights of a certain S.A.
township has a double-pronged reputation, one
end resting on his exemplary piety and the
assiduity with which he runs a Bible-class twice a
week, and the other on his success in taking prizes
for poultry at shows. Last month ho swept the
board of prizes as usual with birds which came up
to an ideal standard in the matter of markings.
Before the show finished the prizes were with-
drawn because another competitor proved to the
judges, by means of a handkerchief and water,
that the Bible-teacher’s roosters had been carefully
painted up for the occasion. Lesson on Jonah as
usual next night.
Anybody who wants to study human
nature ought to run a jeweller’s shop, a large
fashionable establishment in some such thorough-
fare as Oollins-street, Melbourne, where the dudes
most do congregate. Pretty nearly every jeweller
of this kind has at least one customer who is
obviously going fast to perdition, and yon can
measure how fast he goes by his jewellery.
Generally he is a gaudy individual employed by
some bank or financial institution, and he is
voung, and possessed of a high collar, a small
salary, a feeble underjaw for balancing cigars
upon, and a restless eye which seems to be always
on the look-out lest someone should charge at him
from behind. He comes in furtively the first day,
and buys a big, gold bangle, and makes a hazy,
unnecessary explanation to the effect that his
mother has just come in for a lot of money. He
can always be identified by the bangle and the
explanation, and if any benevolent public institu-
tion, or any watchful philanthropist would seize
him at this stage of his career, and cane him
violently, and tell him distinctly that he has no
mother and doesn’t need a bangle, ho might still
be saved. The want of a society for walloping
young men who buy bangles is one of the crying
necessities of the age. The jeweller smiles a cold,
sardonic smile, for he knows that the youth with
the restless eye and the collar will come
back again. And in a few days he does come
back, and states in an excited tone that his
aunt has just died, and left him a pile ; and then
he buys a diamond brooch, and clears out like a
man in great alarm. Later on he turns up again,
and in a more composed manner dumps down £5O
for a big gold watch, and next day he comes round
in a joyous, case-hardened fashion, and speculate*
in the biggest gold chain in the shop. After that
there is generally a long and solemn pause. But
in about a fortnight or three weeks the customer
A NIGHT THOUGHT.
(For The Bulletin.)
The world around is sleeping,
The stars are bright o’erhead,
The shades of myalls weeping
Upon the sward arc spread ;
Among the gloomy pinetops
The fitful breezes blow,
And their murmurs seem the music
Of a song of long ago ;
Soft, passionate, and wailing
Is the tender old refrain—
With a yearning unavailing—
“ Will he no come hack again ?”
The camp-fire sparks are flying
Up from the pine-log’s glow.
The wandering wind is sighing
That ballad sweet and low ;
The drooping branches gleaming
In the firelight, sway and stir ;
And the bushman’s brain is dreaming
Of the song she sang, and her.
And the murmurs of the forest
Ring home to heart and brain,
As in the pine is chorused
“ Will he no come back again ! ’
"THE BREAKER."
Old Punch, who hasn’t made ns smile
These twenty long and weary years,
We put thy numbers on a file
And water them with many tears ;
Thine is the standard British jest
Which lies so heavy on our chest.
There was a time, forgotten lime,-
When brainy men could swallow Punch ;
A “curtain-lecture,” “cut,” or rhyme
Would help them to enjoy their lunch,
Now dull indeed must be the mind
That any fun in thee can fine).
How long shall spiritless cartoon,
And Anglo-Latin paragraph,
Amuse the “ loyal ” cockney loon,
And raise the well-bred booby's laugh !
How long shall “comic” papers make
The comic reader’s stomach ache !
Oh, Tummy, don’t you feel remorse,
To think what yon.’ve been at ?
It’s right enough to back a horse,
But not to baccarat.

Australian missionaries reminds one that in “ The
Serious Family ” Aminadab Sleek asks Captain
Murphy Maguire for half-a-guinea towards the
Borrioboola Glia African Blanket and Topboot
Fund. “Ah,” says the Captain, “ that reminds
me. A poor bricklayer next door fell off a chim-
ney, and I’m collecting a trifle, May 1 put you
down for eighteenpence?” Aminadab : “ I should
be delighted, I’m sure, but it’s not in our line —
not in our line !”

Liberty, published at Perth (W.A.) rises
to ask why a certain citizen should be allowed to
go around the city “hitting people on the head
with an old shirt saturated with water?” Pro-
bably it is because his uncle is related to the
Government.
A lady-correspondent forwards a bright
and lively tale about an imported actress and an
Australian Governor, and sundry troubles which
arose between them about 200 years ago. The
exciting part of the story is where the actress got
into Government House and refused to go out.
She was accompanied by someone who was sup-
posed to be her husband, but who was only a tem-
porary husband borrowed on a steamboat, and
they bad both been at the vice-regal mansion some
time before the row began. Then another im-
ported artist a musician asked what the
Governor meant by introducing him to these
immoral individuals, and the Governor tore his
hair, and sent an aide-de-camp to tell them to
quit. They botli responded cheerfully that his
Excellency “had better peel, and see if he could
chuck them out,” and intimated that there would
be the most awful razzle-dazzle ever seen on these
premises if ho tried. That is how the story goes,
anyhow. When they were gone the viceroy sent
another miserable aide to tell them that their
invitation for the ensuing day was cancelled.
The message he brought back was that the lady
was coming whether her invitation was can-
celled or not, and if necessary she would fight her
way up the stair with a sledge-hammer. She came,
too. She arrived early, and went away late, and
while she was there she pervaded the place,
and froze the whole festivity. She backed respect-
able gentlemen into corners, and talked to them
affably; she bailed up the Governor in a recess,
where he couldn’t escape, and conversed in a
friendly way, and with a heavenly smile on her
countenance ; she collared the aide who had tried
to throw her out, and treated him like a long-lost
brother; and she even seized on the musician who
had given her away, and fraternised with him.
And whenever she pursued and raptured one of
her enemies he thought his last hour had arrived,
and he made such a spectacle of himself in his
efforts to escape that the whole assemblage
laughed. The actress took a fiendish joy in the
business, and at last she would chase an old,
moral, fat man for 20 minutes for the mere
pleasure of arresting him behind a door, and then
going away with an unconscious expression on
her face, as if she had never heard of his existence.
It was a pleasant night, altogether, and the
Governor was worn to rags before it was ended.

Sir Edward Braddon, “K.C.M.G.” (of
which, by the way, they say he is dreadfully
ashamed, and no wonder !), is to be re-appointed
Agent-General for Tasmania for another three
years. He is a clever man, with an iron will, and
has already done the province good service. Sir
Edward is a brother of Miss Braddon, the yellow-
back novelist, but, of course, he can’t help that.

Smith, Jones, Brown, and Robinson have
evidently reached as far as Tasmania. A Brown
was manager of the smashed-up Bank of Y.D.L.;
Brown is the name of the new' Speaker ; a Jones
is governor of Hobart Gaol; a Smith—our old
friend “ Anak ” with the phenomenal legs!—is
town-clerk of Hobart; while a Robinson of most
ladylike mien does the private secretary business
lor Governor Hamilton.

* # *
One of the most amiable, learned, and
unassuming men who ever filled a responsible
situation in N.S. Wales—Mr. C. S. Wilkinson,
Government Geologist—has passed away at the
early age of 41. The cause of his death was cancer
in the stomach, a deadly complaint from which,
we are sorry to hear, two other prominent Sydney
men are now suffering.
* *
Swan, M.L.C., of Brisbane, died the
other day, and his will is published under the title
munificent bequests.” There seems to be some-
thing wrong about this title, for the deceased
gentleman leaves to his wife only a life annuity of
£5OO, to be forfeited if she marries again ; and
gives about £27,000 to the various churches, the
Baptists taking most of the pool. This is the
way many an old gentleman gets his name up after
he is dead. His wife, in many cases, has helped
him to make his pile, or has even made his pile for
him. She has done at least a fair half of the
labour, and has put up with much weariness and
snuffle in the effort; and then she loses her hus-
band and loses all her money save a small
annuity, and is left to spend the rest of her years

church or any other institution; the munificence
consists in the fact that he deprives his
partner, his wife, of the luxuries which
he wouldn’t surrender himself, and gives the
proceeds of her enforced poverty to the cause.
Nobody is munificent who gives away his money
after he is dead, for then it is his money no
longer, and he is merely giving away somebody
else’s property, and shedding somebody else’s
blood in the interests of terewth. And the
capitalist who decrees that his widow shall be
stripped of her income and left utterly penniless
should she marry again is a posthumous tyrant of
a bad description. If his memory will not be
cherished unless under a penalty of £5OO per
annum for not cherishing it—well, it doesn’t say
much for his memory.
After all, we can learn a lot of things
from the alleged barbarians ! According to
the law of Spain (backward and degraded
as this nation is in many particulars) the
wife has a testamentary interest amounting to
one-half of the capitalised value of her husband’s
absolute estate, which she has the right to leave
to her heirs subject to no conditions imposable by
her husband. Of course, a wife dying before her
husband, usually leaves her portion of the estate
to him. This is a customary rule, but it is a
purely optional matter with the dying testator.

The publicity given to the Colston case
has resulted in a similar tragedy at Stratford
(Vie.). One John Ross murdered an old settler
in his garden, for no particular reason except that
“he had to do it.” The man was seized with an
irresistible desire to do the same as Colston and
get his portrait into the hevenink papers.

several in Sydney.

Some of the curiosities of banking. Within
the last few days two institutions in Melbourne
have gone on the road where the defunct bank
goes* and they seem to be strangely tangled up.
At least one of them owns most of the shares in
the other, and it had borrowed £60,000 from the
other on the security of the said share®, and when
the first bank broke it wanted to collar most of
the second bank’s assets by way of calls, and the
second I aide closed up in alarm, and left the
liquidator to sort things out. This is a good
system, if it were only carried to its legitimate
c nclnsion. The proper way to run the business
is to start two banks at once, each with a capital
of a million, all paid up, and let each bank own
the whole of the other’s shares. Then as a natural
result each will have, a million pounds, and neither
will have a cent, and everything will be quite
correct all round.

Youth on the prowl and pleasure at the helm I
TWILIGHT.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
She lingers at the appointed spot,
Her sweet, young face is very fair,
Her dark, dream-haunted eyes are soft—
And sunset’s gold is on her hair.
The twilight’s gathering gloom reflects
The glory of her lovely form.
The matchless grace of attitude—
The full ripe red of rich lips warm.
She listens to the murmuring sounds
Attending twilight shadows deep ;
And soft the purple sunset hues
In beauty o’er her features creep.
Her eat is strained to catch a sound
A footstep’s sound she knows so well
As far across the trees and flowers
The hour that’s chimed, from distant bell,
Throbs softly on the breeze and falls
In sweetest silence, as the bloom
And glory of the sunset pales
Into the twilight’s gathering gloom.
She cares not for the distant chimes
That swell and die along the breeze—
Nor for the murmuring stream that rhymes
In silv’ry incoherencies—
She waits beside the rustic seat
And listens while the deepening night
Blends a resplendent arch of stars
Above the sunset’s dying light.
The lengthening shadows force the gloom
Athwart the flowers and pathways dark
The gilded moonlight creeping slow
Rocks like a stranded fairy bark-
And still he comes not! Dull and slow
The big, bright teardrops glistening fall;
The fitful shadows darker grow
Deep in the silence—that is all!
But hark ! his footstep sounds at last !
Her heart with rapture leaps to meet
The gladsome music cf his voice,
The echo of his hastening feet.
Fond foolish love that could not wait !
Her bright head on his shoulder slips—
And ah ! her soul in ecstasy
His kiss draws from her pouting lips !
“ 1 was detained, my love,” he said,
By that which would not brook delay ;
Impatiently my fond heart chafid
Ere I could tear myself away ! ”
Oh, doubtful cause of his delay !
Ne’er as he said to part Till death,
Alas, for all he had to say
She smelt the whisky in his breath !


We go abroad for news. Says an English
exchange : “ Australia furn'shes a novel invention
in the shape of glass tubes projecting before rail-
way trains, which if broken by an obstacle apply
an electric movement to the brakes, bringing the
train immediately to a standstill.”

Probably the most notable of Australian
aborigines has just “ gone over ” in the person of Johnny
Mullagh, the celebrated cricketer. Mullagh in his day
was a grand all-round athlete, and his fine physical pro-
portions and intelligent speech tempted the Saturday
Review to dub him “ the finest specimen of a savage ever
seen in England.” Mullagh, who spoke English as clearly
as an educated white, was looked upon as a very superior
person by his fellow blacks; in fact, he was generally
addressed as “ Mr.”Mullagh. Bullocky, another of the
team taken to England by Lawrence, was one day playing
against his compatriot at Coleraine, and, as usual, had
been indulging in the rosy. Consequently he swiped a
little too soon at one of Mullagh’s tempters, and the
result proved fatal. When passing the bowler, Mullagh
said, “ What made you slog at that one, Bullocky ? ”
“ Well, Mr. Mullagh,” was the response, “I bin see two
balls, and 1 hit at the wrong one.” Mullagh was the last
of his tribe. Though quite black he was comely, his pro-
file being of a pronounced Semitic type.
# * ~ *
Cable announces that the English bookmakers
have been ordered to clear out of France forthwith. The
fraternity of Anglo-French commission-agents, as they
called themselves, started business.in Boulogne more than
twenty years age, “Boy ’ Wright, Webster and Johnny
Gideon being about the first to send out circulars and
price-lists from across the Channel. An attempt to carry
on the game in Edinburgh—after the London police had
put the Betting Act in force, and ousted all advertising
list-men from their original offices—was tolerated for a
short time, but eventually a Scotch law was brought to
bear upon the case, and these bloated exiles from the
Fleet-street district had to find a home amongst the
mounseers. Providence has been very good to them all
this time. So much English money was sent across that
they became, through their home agents, the biggest
regular customers of the London bookmakers, that is to
say, their agents would constantly receive instructions
to “back in” the horses which were overlaid to large
amounts on the book at Boulogne or Paris. Their purely
local harvest, too, must have been considerable every
year, because the British “bookey” has always reigned
supreme on French racecourses, where his disgust for
betting in “ naps ” instead of “quids” was expressed in
lurid adjectives. Probably'there is no intention on the
part of the authorities to shut out English layers of odds
from French meetings. They will, we presume, still cross
the Channel to attend the Grand Prix and other big
events, bringing their brass-bound volumes with them.
But it would seem that the resident leviathan, the tissue
list-issuer, is not to be tolerated any longer, and unless
he can find another resting-place in Brussels or Amster-
dam, this particular variety of gambling institution will
soon fade out of recollection, like the tables at Monaco.
Nevertheless, backing horses will go on much about the
same as usual, until horse-racing is suppressed, and the
lamb and the “lamber” lie down together. Meanwhile
the cable announces again that the French authorities
having reconsidered their decision the bookmakers have
not been expelled. Thus the above is all lost sentiment
and waste reflection.

Seeing that Sullivan’s theatrical season in Aus-
tralia can’t be making his fortune or enriching his
manager beyond the dreams of avarice, the sporting
world racks its fuddled brain to understand why “ Jonel”
doesn’t drop the drama for a while and fill in the interval
by fighting for £2OOO purses and things, the manager to
take a share of the profits. The excuse that he can
11 make more dollars at acting ” avails them no longer. It
won't wash. Victory in another big fight would mean
money in the drammer anon, and the Slugger doesn’t
fight, not so much because he suspects that any good
man would kill him in the third round, as because he
knows that a certain defeat would knock the last spark
of life out o? bis show, anyway.

and Donald Dinnie want to arrange a partnership with

Criffo is to fight Burge (the sire of seven
children), who has shown that he can come down to
feather-weight. Griffo is thus going to “fight his
father,” and will probably meet his first licking. The
cast-iron man, whom Qeo. Dawson couldn’t knock out,
and who held up his jaw for M'Kenzie to punch at, isn’t
likely to be troubled by either Griffo’s blows or his
endurance.
* * *
The Age stupidly took Judge Hamilton to
task for his pertinent summing-up of Mr. John F.
Sheridan’s midnight attack on Middleton. Judge
Hamilton went right down to the root of the matter
when he said: “The assault had been committed in a
manner that would not commend itself to any manly
mind. Hitting a man a blow from behind might be con-
sidered heroic in America, where the defendant came
from, but it was not so in Australia.” This is what
the Age terms “indulging in gratuitously offensive
expressions against a nation as against an indivi-
dual,” whereas the allusion was not quite so
gratuitous as it looked. A very brief acquain-
ance with the land of J. L. Sullivan would convince an
Age journalist that there are blots on the "Murrikan
civilisation of the smart Yankee. He is apt to be nastier
in quarrel and more promiscuous in his modes of wreaking
vengeance than the average Australian, or the decently
educated Britisher. He may not arm himself with kicking
clogs like the Lancashire lad, but he still hankers after a
“ shooter,” and his notions of chivalry are bad for the man
who has left his “iron” at home. In fact, there is a dis-
position amongst the Bohemians of the West to put.
blackguardism on a pedestal and worship the man who
draws first blood any way he darned well chooses. A
good many other Bohemians are built the same way.
When Mr. Hamilton touched upon the characteristics
of Widdy O’Brien’s “stoush and run” policy he spoke
like a man of the world and a brother, and was no more
gratuitously offensive than judges are frequently com-
pelled to be, or daily newspapers constantly are, without
being compelled. When a raw Italian sticks a knife into
an Australian citizen, the Judge always reminds him that
such customs of his country are not approved of here. As
for the Age, so thoughtful for the feelings of America,
the dear creature is never happy unless it is insulting the
Irish nation point-blank, or by innuendo.
* # #
The new Bendigo Racing Club made a modest
but very successful commencement last week. As the
stakes varied from a “ tenner ” to £25, no light was shed
on the big coming events.
# » *
The programme of the Western Australian Turf
Club for its New Year’s meeting (January Ist and 4th,
1892) has been issued. The first day’s events are:—
Maiden Plate, of 50sovs.; Nursery Stakes, cf bOsovs. ;
Selling Race, of SOsovs.; Perth Cup, a handicap of 400sovs.,
2 miles ; Ladies’ Purse and the Flying Stakes, each of
50sovs. Second day Metropolitan Hurdles, of 70sovs.;
West Australian Derby, of 75sovs.; Railway Stakes, a
handicap of 17550v5.; City Purse and Farewell Handicap,
each of 50sovs.; and the Queen’s Plate, of lOOsovs., 3 miles.
The amount of this last event is contributed by the
Government of the province as an inducement to owners
and breeders. The club owns a nice little course about
four miles from the city, and which is connected with the
latter by rail.
* # #
Jem Hall, the Australian middle-weight
puncher, has been seriously stabbed in the throat, “in
self-defence,” at Michigan (LLS.), by “Parson” Davies,
his manager. The knife and the pistol bring down even
pugilists to the physical level of ordinary beings.
# # *
At Brisbane Exhibition, Mr. Fenwick’s Pasha
won the hunter’s high jump at sft. bin., and, continuing,
got over bft. Jin. with 12st. 21b. on his back. The Brisbane
jumper's performances have been discounted by those at
Melbourne Agricultural Show, where Spondulix got over
bft. 9in. (carrying list. 71b.); Cedric, bft. 7in.; and High-
lander, bft. 4in. The American jumper has a
record of 7ft. 2Jin.

Mitchell and Corbett are talking fight. Nothing
will come of it; the Englishman is the most persistent
traveller on the post extant. Sullivan can’t come near
him.

Poor Johnnie Mullagh, the dusky cricketer,
was the last of his tribe. It’s hard to find a Victorian
aboriginal who isn’t the last of his tribe, nowadays.

at suburban race-meetings.

! Editor Bulletin, — “E. K.” wants to know in
j what year Bob M‘Laron came to Australia. I was in the
next cabin to him on the voyage out. We left Liverpool
I on New Year s Day, 1857, in the good ship James Baines,
' and arrived in Hobson’s Bay on March 15, 1857. Bob was
then from California, and when I left for Maoriland, in
’62, he was a policeman in the Main Hoad in Ballarat. I
never heard of his being known by the name of Thomp-
son.—'Tims. W. Wkbb, Swan Hotel, Richmond, Melbourne.
August 24, 1891.
