Our thirst for knowledge isn’t great,
We do not itch or burn
To learn the secrets of the State—
They give us small concern ;
We don't aspire to read the stars
That twinkle through the night,
We simply hang around the bars
Or shops that “ kid ” to sell cigars,
And ask “ Is Carbine right?”
The world it may be square or round.
We do not want to know,
(You see, we put our little pound
Upon him long ago)—
And when Protection is discuss’t
We never join the fight;
In Parkes or Dibbs we put no trust—
All questions fill us with disgust
Save this : “ Is Carbine right ?”
The Labour-party’s prospects ne’er
Excite our weary brain ;
If Federation’s in the air,
Well, there it can remain,
Let others have ideas sublime,
And hanker after “ light,”
Whilst we, who pen this funny rhyme,
Will merely ask, from time to time,
“ Is Carbine keeping right ?”
FROM SYDNEY TO MELBOURNE ON
BICYCLES.
INCIDENTS BY THE WAY.
Mr. J. Pearson, of King-street Arcade, tha
well-known cycling tourist, when riding over to
Melbourne with his friend, Mr. A. G. Chapman,
relates several adventures they met with on their
long journey:
For four days after leaving Sydney terribly web
weather was experienced, and many of the water-
courses, which in the summer time arc nearly dry,
were swollen so much that they had great difficulty
in crossing some of them.
On one occasion they were compelled to strip
and carry their machines across, the current being
so strong that they were in imminent danger of
being swept away. Although they were for
several days riding in wet clothes no dire results
accrued, as on each evening after retiring they
took the precaution to rub themselves down with
St. Jacobs oil, which Mr. Pearson always carries
with him on his tours, finding it a capital remedy
for stiffness and soreness of the muscles, as well
as a preventative and cure for painful ailments
induced by cold.
After getting over the dividing range the
weather changed from wet and cold to excessive
heat. On one day especially the thermometer was
98 in the shade.
Numerous snakes were met with on the journey,
passing several coiled up in the middle of the road.
One monster, over six feet long, caused them some
trouble, as in trying to avoid it the two cyclists
collided, the result of which was a bruised wrist
and a swollen ankle, but the application of the
ever ready St. Jacobs oil prevented any bad
effects. They reached Melbourne safely, averaging
64 miles per day, and received a great reception
from their brother wheelmen of the southern
capital.
St. Jacobs Oil may be obtained from any
respectable chemist, storekeeper, or dealer.
Demand St. Jacobs Oil, and positively refuse to
accept any of the numerous substitutes which are
frequently tendered in its stead.
ANTI-MACASSARS.
These are to some extent a relic of a semi-
barbarous past, but many of them are still exceed-
ingly beautiful, and of great domestic importance.
They have exercised a soothing influence in a large
number of households for many years, and are
frequently carried about by the proprietary, en-
tangled upon the back buttons of his coat, if the
cat has not already dragged them off the chairs to
make a home for her young family in an obscure
corner of the floor. Bad tempered men have been
known to make hasty and unfeeling remarks about
the general uncertainty of anti-macassars, and
what they conceive to be their proper value in
the sphere of domestic economy, but the article
is nevertheless useful for making impromptu
dolls for the younger children, for stuffing rat-
holes, for chasing the wily cockroach to his
lair, and frequently for exciting the envy of a
neighbor and the comments of a whole street. The
anti macassar has also a decided advantage over
every other artie’e classified as belonging to the
family linen-chest —it can be hung out to dry in
the most unprotected spot without danger of
being stolen. Nobody ever yet stole one con-
sciously, and principally because—no matter how
lovely—they are of small commercial value.
Their area is uncertain and color various, >
depending almost entirely on the tastes of the
owner. They are made of many fabrics, and in
millions of patterns. A very favorite pattern for
the modern anti macassar is said to be produced
with a bone needle and a hank of sail twine, the
stitch being chain 3, drop 1, purl 6, tie the ends
together, then reverse and repeat to the size
required. This is the one supposed to be referred
to by Byron in the couplet—
In virtues nothing earthly could surpass her,
Save fhine ‘‘incomparable oil,” “Macassar!”
And the quotation happily reminds us that
there is one even more incomparable oil than that,
the beauties of which have been much testified to
of late, and that is Luxene, the new illuminant,
which stands alone as a light giver, as a house-
wives’ friend, and as a guarantee against accidents
and danger by land or sea. Luxene is the very
perfection of an illuminant for all these and many
other reasons, not the least of which is its economy
in everyday use. Messrs. J. Barlow and Co.,
T. W. Eady and Son, Mason. Bros., Ltd.,
Tillock and Co., D. Mitchell and Co., J. G. Hanks
and Co., J. Keep and Sons, Holdsworth,
MTherson and Co., M'Lean Bros, and Bigg, Ltd.,
Fred. Butcher, J. G. Barrett, and Robert Miller,
Sydney; J. Ireland, R. Hall and Son, and David
Miller, Newcastle, will be pleased to- answer
inquiries and furnish all information about it.

We have received, a new novel entitled
“ Making his Pile; A Colonial Story,” published
by Mason, Firth, and M‘Cutcheon, and written
by Justin Charles M'Cartie. The name on the
title-page creates an impression, at the outset,
that the work is by another Justin —but this mis-
apprehension very soon wears off. The new
volume has an elegant yellow cover, showing the
moon rising over a waste of waters, with an em-
bittered bank-clerk and another misanthrope on
the desolate shore in the foreground ; and the
whole thing weighs about lialf-a-pound. Ihe
contents relate to sundry young men of lofty
character and irreproachable morals who come out
from England and settle in Dunedin, where they
are fawned upon and grovelled to by the entire
population, because the local inhabitant always
grovels to the new-chum, and worships him above
every other thing on this earth. These young men,
owing to their high principles and other causes, are
the only visible oases in the black, sin-smothered,
soul-blasted waste of iniquity which pervades
all Maoriland, and they have a hard row to
hoe in making head against the unclean thing
generally. In the 22nd chapter all the natives
bolt with the cash unanimously, and leave the prin-
cipal good young man from “Home” naked, save
for his rectitude. Then he takes up arms against
a sea of troubles, and fights his way through a
whole nation of low, base miscreants till he gets
to the wharf, and leaves Maoriland for ever.
There is nothing in the book save the irreproach-
able young man from “Home,” and the blasted
local deceiver. The tale is plain and harmless;
the hero is remarkably like a left-off person by
Ouida, and the horrid “ kawlinist ” is partly an
impossible character out of Eever, and partly the
cheap and greasy money-lender out of Trollope s
“Three Clerks.” Most of him comes to a bad
end. The other man marries a heroic and beauti-
ful girl employed in a refreshment-room, and lives
happily ever afterwards.
The London & Sydney Tea Co. supply squatters
and farmers at wholesale prices.
Dear Bulletin, —I have taken a large interest
in you from way back in Maoriland, and have read you
right through. But on one thing we must disagree.
Sound or cronk, a man who slays his fellow-creature in
cold gall should be put to death ; he is not fit to live,
his remains, however offensive, are better guests at a
wake than his live carcase, and the writer would rather
sleep with Colston in the early stages of decay than when
possessed of life and vigour and a meat-axe. I like your
paper because [marmalade omitted —Ed.]— but you will
never persuade me that capital punishment is bar-
barous. I hold that a homicidal maniac is much
better company with a saucer of salt on his bosom, a
tallow-candle illuminating his noble brow, and a raw
potato keeping his chin in place. If I had my way I
would kill every one of them on sight. You should be in
» lunatic-asylum for a year —not that I want to see
you there — to know' something about murderers
doing the Queen’s pleasure. If it be any pleasure to the
Queen to know that these ruffians are as crafty as a fox,
and that they will brain a warder with a shovel or broom,
just for the fulfilment of a grudge, and then laugh it off
as a good joke, the Queen must have a lot of fun. Do you
know what ‘‘ During the Queen’s pleasure ” means ? No.
Well, I will tell vou. It means that a criminal “fly”
enough (and they are “ fly”) to listen to the chaplain and
attend prayers with a profound expression of humility
and repentance will be turned loose in about ten years, a
worse savage than when he was put there. Don t j ou
make any methylated error about this ! It is a cast-iron
fact, and it can’t be softened down to the tenacity of
horse-nails by any specious argument you can advance.
The jury in Colston’scase were quite right. Sane orinsane,
the man was better dead. You can’t get over it. If you
own a mad dog or a glandered horse—which, thanks to
your exceptionally healthy climate you never will own
would you not destroy him in the speediest fashion ? You
would shovel him away into cold earth as an incurable
nuisance, and now you want me and a hundred thousand
others to believe that a lunatic who slays because his grey
brain matter likes it that way, is fit to live “during her
Majesty’s pleasure,” or the pleasure of anybody. ,No,
dear Bulletin, break his neck by all means, and break it
quick. As I said before, you should have 12 months-oh,
no, what I was going to say was, experience. _ About half
the people now in gaol should have died young. If some
of them have been flogged they only got the wrong end
of the rope—the noose end is what they wanted.
Humanity to one gory prisoner means gross inhumanity
to all the rest of creation. The said g. p. is a standing
menace to society while he draws breath. I suppose every
creature that draws breath is an animal. Yes. Well, this
is the worst kind of an animal, and the sooner he ceases
to draw breath the better for all humans.—I am &c.,
J. M. W. Dunedin (Maoriland), August 11, 1891. [Our
correspondent is a stern utilitarian. He would poll-axe
his paralysed father and visit with the gallows the
demented victim of puerperal fever. Prevention is better
than cure. You can trust no lunatic ; therefore, all mad
people should be slain. —Ed. B.]

Personal Items.
Duffy is now the only retired Victorian
Speaker alive.
Strange stories are told of Joe Chamber-
lain’s little pleasures.
After all, Lalor’s relations seem to be
rather ashamed of the Eureka Stockade. Hardly
respectable, you know.
We hear on unreliable authority that
Parkes’ new book will be styled “ Great Men
Who Have Known ME !”
Professor M‘Coy is the only one of the
original staff of the Melbourne University now
left. He has held his post for 37 years.
Sydney University is rather weak in its
English department. Dr. Cullen, for instance,
talks of “ substituting this instead o/that.”
Stead, the German Emperor and Sir Gus.
Harris have taken all the nasty Tranby-Croft
taste out of the British mouth. Arise Sir W. T.
Stead !
Sir Alfred Stephen is credited with saying
that Sir William Manning became an advocate
when he became a Judge, and a Judge when lie
became a politician.
In the death of that fine writer, James
Runciman (“ John A’Dreams ”), “the fatal 37”
is illustrated for the thousandth time. Runci-
inan was in his 38th year.
The title of that venerable dodo, the Duke
of Cleveland, has become extinct. His ground-
rents didn’t die with him, however. Titles to
land are inextinguishable.
Crown Princess Stephanie of Austria,
widow of the young fiend who murdered his
“tart” and killed himself, is said to bo an in-
veterate punter at Monte Carlo.
The Standard (Mclb.) reporter referred
to Colston, on the drop, as “ standing on the lips
of Eternity.” He probably formed his conception
of Eternity from studying his own mouth in a
glass.
“Mr. Deodatus William Helm Johnstone
Neilson O’Neil Neil ” is the name of the engineer
who ordered the twelve locomotives which are
causing so much excitement in N.S.W. political
circles.
The celebrated English lunacy expert, Dr.
Savage (author of “ A Monograph on Insanity”),
is of opinion that Rousseau was a person of
unsound mind, who ought to have been confined
in an asylum.
Nicholls, well remembered on the Bal-
larat Star, and now editor of the Hobart
Mercury, was one of the unfortunates in the Bank
of Van Diemen’s Land smash—£looo hard savings
all “gone bung.”
Judge Docker, ofN.S.W., thinks he ought
to be a sound lawyer because his parents christened
him “ Brougham.” But then it was said of the
Brougham that if he but knew a little law he
would know a little of everything.
Gordon-Oumming’s bride was described as
“ daughter of the late Commodore Garner, of the
New York Yacht Cmh That's much about the
same as being the daughter of a majaw in the
Salvation Army, or a captain of a fire-brigade, we
fancy.
Andy Kelly, M.P. (N.S.W.), will be
pleased to learn that the Volksraad of the Trans-
vaal Republic have expunged the “bell-topper”
from a rule of order which declared that “ mem-
bers shall be dressed in black, with a white tie
aid 11 ick silk hat.”
Attorney Tom Slattery (N.S.W.) tells a
story of an old countryman of his own whose last
will and testament he had drawn out. Pat waited
on Tom a month or two afterwards to have an
alteration made, as he “found he had left himself
without even a chair to sit on.”
A certain N.S. Wales M.P. is keen at
retort, but sometimes strikes a snag. To him
Victor Daley once : “ John, when you die, please
leave me your skull.” “ Want to travel on my
brains?” promptly enquired John, “No,” said
the gentle Daley, “ I want it for a spittoon.”
Mr. Robert Scarlett, “ The Private Secre-
tary,” says he was to get £5O a month for editing
Dailey’s Memoirs, but when the book will appear
is not notified. It was generally understood that
I rank Hutchinson, the well-known journalist
had been appointed as the literary executor of his
lifelong friend.

ot the signatures are those of people who decamped
Irom Maonland leaving their autographs affixed to
unpaid documents.

Judge M‘Farland still takes his little
snack on Sydney District Court bench, though
the sight of a judicial dignitary with his mouth
distended like that of Jem in “Lights o’ Lon-
don,” is certainly not awe-inspiring. Judge M‘E.
has a follower in his “eccentricity” in Judge
Richmond, of N.Z., who regularly has “afternoon
tea brought to him while he is in court.
Young Earl Dudley’s fiancee is Miss
Rachel Gurney (daughter of Gurney, of Overend,
Gurney and Co.) who is the adopted daughter of
the Duchess of Bedford. The Duke, it is said,
will give her £lOO,OOO as dot, which sounds un-
likely, as he is the meanest old hunks alive. Miss
Gurney is very handsome, very accomplished, and
is one of the best amateur singers in England.
George Ooppin intends to move in the
Victorian Council that potatoes shall be “exempt
from the sales prohibited on Sunday.” The
veteran speaks feelingly. There is an ancient
tradition that lie always carries a potato in Lis
trousers-pocket to ward olf the gout. Picture
his position if he felt a warning twinge after his
Sunday’s dinner —and never a tater in the house !
Another of Tummy’s friends is being-
shown-up for his infamous neglect of his wretched
tenantry. A medical officer recently reported
that the “ wood huts ” on Sir Tatton Sykes’
estate “are absolutely unfit for human habita-
tion.” There is just one grain of excuse for Sir
Sykes. He is understood to have partially ruined
himself in entertaining the Prince and his grass-
hopper-like mob.
Writing of Father Walsh, who died
recently at Port Macquarie, N.S.W., a country
paper tells how one of his Lithgow parishioners
thus delivered herself to him on one occasion:—
“Yer riv’rence, that ould Misthtress Kelly up
there beyant does be saying that the clergy should
be practising and not be praching so much ; and
she said, bad luck to the same old woman for a
long tongue she has, that last Monday as yo was
going to Sydney, yer riv’rence tuk a bottle of
porther at wan dhrink for yer lunch ; and I up
and told her she was a liar.” Without a smile
came the reply in Father Walsh’s best brogue ;
“ True for you, Mrs. Rafferty, my good sowl; it
was what you called her that she was : for I tuk
two !”
MISS CREO STANLEY
(The first Lady Delegate to the N.S.W. Trades and
Labour Council)
Lady Jersey was not always content to
write meek little poems. Somebody has been
good enough to send us a copy of the Banbury
(Eng.) Guardian of July 1, 1886, containing a
howling oration by iier ladyship on the necessity
for Toiy coercion in Ireland. It is garnished with
many flowers of oratory about the incapacity of
the old Irish-Parliament—which, by-the bye, was
an English Protestant Parliament, only Lady
Jersey smothers that fact; about the alleged
period when Irishmen boiled English people to
death—which reminds ns also of how the Anglo-
Saxon of the same period used to torture political
offenders to deatli by piling paving stones on their
stomachs ; and other matters. Also she complains
that Gordon of Khartoum was killed by the
“poisonous breath”-of Mr. Gladstone, which
seems rather steep, unless Gladstone habitually
breathes by submarine cable. Altogether it is a
daisy of a speech, two columns long ; and it winds
up with poetry about the flag of our fathers.
How long is Melbourne to be hidebound ?
The week before last we had a £5 fine for selling
twopenn’oth of potatoes, on Sunday, to a poor
woman. A few weeks ago was another case, £5
fine, for a bottle of gingerbeer, supplied to a
little boy, out walking with his parents, on a hot,
dusty Sunday.
A tragic event has cast a gloom ove r
fashionable society in Paris. There was a high-
toned dinner party in progress, and in tho middle
of it the host’s big dog came in, and genially ate
two immense mouthfuls out of the vital parts of
the principal guest, who died a few minutes after-
wards. This new feature in dinner-parties has
created a feeling of depression in polite circles.
That Unknown Quantity—Happiness.
Euclid might have achieved a greater fame than
he now holds, had lie propounded this problem—
“ Given a straight line, find happiness.” And
then all the world would have thought itself into
a bald and unconvincing condition in trying to
solve the problem. Possibly the eminent Geome-
trician did state this proposition in his last books,
but these were burned in the conflagration of an
ancient library, and we have not time just now to
rake over the debris. For all that, man, from
the very beginning of the world, has tacitly set
himself to solve the problem, and has barely suc-
ceeded until the capacity for enjoying his know
ledge had been buried with his forgotten years,
and disregarded opportunities. So then logically
with,the foregoing, man is happy only when he is
un.onscious of his happiness. Happiness is mo.tly
a question of health, winch again is the well-being
of organs, which the owner is hardly conscious of
possessing. The best news in the world will not
make a hypochondriac dance, nor a rheumatic
martyr sing—the effect on his mind is joyous, but
the mental joy is counteracted by the material
misery. How many men have we seen praying
for death —a visitor terrible even in the most
alluring form, and clad in the most peaceful garb,
because everything they have used has failed, and
they think that nothing can be done to save them
from impending death? They read this, or are
advised by friends to test the sterling virtues of
Warner’s Safe Cure. They do so, and in a few
weeks the sallowness of the invalid has vanished,
and the pink of health is being forced into their
cheeks. Well it is happiness to sec those respited
prisoners enjoying their m wly obtained health, it
is happiness to them and to you if you have a
heart for the troubles of other men. William
Hill, a railway gatekeeper at St. Arnaud, (Vic.),
was in such a state of living death, and to his
happiness on regaining health, let his own words
testify. Here is his letter :—Dead bot yet Alive.
St. Arnaud, Victoria.—l was a martyr to chronic
gout for a term of 34 years, during which time I
spent over two thousand pounds on different
doctors, having to pay £3 and £4 a visit in the
early days of the colony. I also tried change of
air in different parts of the colony, but never got
any permanent relief. I was given up on several
occasions by the doctor, and once he told my wife
I could not possibly live till the morning. 1 have
been confined to my bed over 21) months, part of
the time quite insensible, but in spite of all I
managed to live on in misery to myself and
every one about me—dead but yet alive. It was
during this critical time my wife read of some
wonderful cures by the use of Warner’s safe cure,
and was determined I should try it as a last re-
source. The doctor at this time had given me up,
and said he could do nothing for me. I began the
use of safe cure, having to pay five shillings and
sixpence per bottle, but money was no object
where life was concerned ; after a few weeks I
was able to sit up, and continued to improve. I
continued its use until I have taken thirty or forty
bottles of Warner’s safe cure, and several vials of
Warner’s safe pills. lam now able to get about
and enjoy life once more. lam G 7 years of age.
My friends tell me I must have an iron constitu-
tion, but I tell them it is owing to Warner’s safe
cure, and I shall never cease to be grateful for tho
benefits I derived from its use.—William Hill,
Gate Keeper on the Railway. What further
proof is desired ?
Black Tommy,” the aborigine now under
committal for tomahawking and criminally
assaulting a woman (since dead) at West
Maitland, N.S. Wales, had, only a few days
prior to the offence, been liberated from the
local gaol after serving a sentence of six months for
a somewhat similar crime. Previous to that he
had “done” 10 years for horse-stealing. He is
an athletic fellow of gigantic proportions, and a
bit of a “pug.” Tommy is an expert stone-
cutter. Curiously enough almost every Australian
b'ack in the gaols of N.S.W. has been convicted
cf an offence against decency, the only notable ex-
ception being “ Tommy Pyan,” who is finishing a
term at Darlinghm st for gaol breaking and nearly
killing the arresting policeman.
Editor Bulletin,—You ask how the pro-
hibitionist teetotallers would deal with the Aus-
tralian wine-industry. Those knowing the views
and aims of the cranks and hypocrites who form
the backbone of the “ temperance ” movement well
know that they would deal with it summarily.
It is, however, doubtful w hether they will ever
luiye the power of deciding the question. The
failure of their pet scheme, local option, in Canada
and certain parts of the States, has produced such
a split in the temperance camp that it will be a
long time ere they can put forward an effectual
solution from their point of view of the drink
Problem. The object of these remarks is to point
out a means by which the liquor question and ti e
wine industry can be simultaneously and satisfac-
torily dealt with. I maintain that theGothenburgh
licensing system could be easily applied in Aus-
tralia. In Sweden beer is subject to no legal
restrictions either as to sale or manufacture.
Were the Gothenburgh plan tried here we could
have free trade in wine, while the profits arising
from the sale of beer and spirits could be devoted
to national objects, such as the maintenance of
police, gaols, asylums, and other institutions
brought into existence principally thro' gh the
present liquor traffic. Moreover, the traffic would
be a pure one, for the vendor would be a public
servant paid by salary, and, unlike our friend
Bung, would not, by adulteration and other
methods, hasten to accumulate a fortune so that
he and Ins wife might pick a bone and shake a leg
at Government House.— Yours, E.

for the A.M.P. Society at Auckland (M.L.), has

Balmaceda, the defeated Chilian Pre-
sident, is described as a very fair-haired man of
over six feet with a dash of Indian blood whence
comes his cruelty.
The Maoriland Treasurer lately signed his
name at the bottom of a Maori advertisement,
“ Monita Whakahiri o te mone o te kirone,
which, being interpreted, is “The Minister who
makes the money of the colony fly.”
Sydney Illustrated News, apropos to
some remarks in the Review of Reviews, avers
that “ Parkes has not been defeated, but has a
working majority at his back.” Just as we read
this we looked out of the window and saw an
unwilling drunk being hustled quod-wards by
three able-bodied, policemen. That man, thought
we, had also at his back a working majority.
The Germans appear to have been taking
a hand in the Chilian trouble ; anyhow, the war
seems to have been used as a preliminary canter-
ground for Mannlicher rifles and smokeless
powder. The result of the attack made by Bal-
maceda’s comparatively big force upon entrenched
positions defended by the insurgents falls in with
Archibald Forbes’ opinion that smokeless powder
and repeating rifles now render assaults hopeless.
At one of “General” Booth's English
farewell meetings he asked those who had found
faith to stand up. All stood except the members
of the Press, whose attitude was adroitly inter-
preted by Booth as an evidence of a mistaken
desire to remain impenitent. Yet it was more
probably an outward sign of that lack of faith
in anything or anybody, which appears to be the
inevitable outcome of a journalistic life. And as
in England so in Australia. At none of the many
church-assemblies, etc., attended by the present
writer in his capacity as a reporter has he ever
known the newspaper men to remain while
prayers were going on.
Mr. T. S. Dick writes from Burnside,
S.A., to say that The Bulletin in ascribing
the invention of the sheep-shearing machine,
known as the “Wolseley,” to a Victorian
engineer, is wrong; that the invention is
Mr. Wolseley’s, who worked at it, to our corres-
pondent’s personal knowledge, 12 years ago; and
that Mr. Wolseley exhibited it in a primitive state
11 years ago to a number of gentlemen including
Mr. William Waugh, lately one of the holders of
Dungaleer Station, and Captain Fisher, now S.M.
of Sydney. Our correspondent adds that, from
what Mr. Wolseley told him years ago, the sub-
ject has engaged that gentleman’s attention for
a quarter of a century.

The sea-serpent has fallen through again
in the usual way. That is to say, one ship recently
sighted the sea-serpent, and a day or two later
another ship sighted what might have been the
sea-serpent only it was a tree instead, and because
the tree wasn’t a sea-serpent it is unanimously
resolved that there isn’t any such serpent at all.
And, in the same way, we were aroused the other
night by a horrid row in the back yard, and con-
cluded that it was Parkes, but when we went out
it was only a goat, and seeing that goat wasn’t
Parkes, therefore it is evident that there isn’t any
Parkes whatever.
Dear Bulletin, —Some obnoxiously-in-
quisitive friends of mine want to know if the
contractor for Sydney G.P.O. clock will lose his
£1000 deposit for not having completed his con-
tract within the specified time—viz., nine months.
A man well known to theN.S.W. mechanical pro-
fession went to look at the plans and specifications
when tenders were called some 20 months ago, and
his attention was particularly drawn by the clerk
in charge to the £1000 forfeiture clause. It was
an impossibility, he reckoned, to finish the clock
as per specifications in the time stated, so the
would-be contractor refrained from tendering.
Suggestive, this. Anyhow, what about the £1000?
—Yours, A. D., Mittagong.
Dean Bulletin. —lie your printed, wish
that public libraries would cease taking your red-
covered paper so as to increase the number of
your subscribers : —For some years I ran a paper
near the border of two of the colonies. A new
farming district, 20 miles away from the office,
furnished about 50 subscribers, all good marks, who
regularly paid their 24s. per annum. A little
township grew there and it was decided to build a
mechanics’ institute by public subscription. On
the strength of the place supporting the paper t
subscribed three guineas. Presently the institute
was opened and at the first meeting of the
Committee they asked for a free copy of my paper
as it was for a public institution. Bequest
complied with, and the result was that during the
following month 23 of the most regular payers
stopped their paper because “they though; it
was their duty to support their new institute.”
Thus was charity rewarded. —Yours G. A.
Secuked Deposits.— In view of the promised
legislation to regulate the business of those finan-
cial institutions which receive money on deposit
from the public, we are pleased to notice that a
step in the right direction has been taken by the
Universal Land and Deposit Bank, Limited, who,
in a little pamphlet entitled “Mutual Banking,”
have called attention to a few points of practical
interest to all bank depositors. The main idea of
the above company appears to be that the
depositors, not the shareholders, should be the
first to receive the benefit of the profits made by
the bank, whilst, by the issue of debentures, they
(the depositors) obtain a first lien over all the
assets of the concern and are in possession of a
collateral security equal to the sum deposited,
which, by the way, bears interest. The scheme
is entirely novel, and we think the originators
deserve the support of the public in their efforts
to improve the position of the depositor. We'
commend the above-mentioned pamphlet to the
attention of our readers who contemplate invest-
ing their money in this manner. It is forwarded
post free to any address by the bank, whose office
is at 301 Pitt-street, Sydney.*

The Evolution of a Journalist.
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
Editor Bulletin,—
Sir, —I have long desired to embrace a literary career,
and niy friends have advised me to apply to you to see
if there might not be an opening on your staff. I anl 19
years of age and a junior teacher in a State school. My
friends assure mo that I have Considerable literary
ability. I myself feel that t have a good command of
English, and can write grammatically and in good style.
It has always been my ambition to become a reporter,
and, if you have no vacancy which 1 can fill, will you
kindly advise me how to gain a footing in the journalistic
world ? All niy friends assure me that I would be a
success as a reporter and writer, and they admire my
essays Very much indeed. Please reply by return of post,
as t am eager to change my vocation.—Yours, &0., John
Obammcm.
The above is a paraphrase from memory of a
genuine letter recently received, and a fair sample
of many constantly coming to hand. It is with
no view of discouraging latent literary genius that
I pen this screed, for a soul with a mission will
fulfil that mission against all odds. But I desire
to dissipate some of the fen-fires,, some of the
jack-o’-lanterns, that danoe mockingly among
journalistic marshes, and glorify to deluded
imaginations the life of the reporter.
An ordinary press hack is about as miserable a
slave as the world holds. The outsider regards
the scribe with awe, or reverence, or envy. With
awe, under the impression that the pallid reporter
is “a power in the land,” whereas he is only as
mere an automaton as a caligraph. With rever-
ence, under the belief that the brassy interviewer
of Ministers of the Crown and other so called
“personages,” is on intimate terms with these
notabilities, Whereas the said Ministers and
“ personages ” regard him as a superior sort of
flunkey, created to be humbugged and fooled by
masked condescension, large whiskys, and free
cigars. With envy, under the delusion that the
bottled-nosed frequenter of theatres, or attendant
at champagne luncheons and banquets, is a
Welcome and honoured companion and guest of
the alleged “great ones ” of the earth, whereas he
Is only a tolerated scout and advertiser of
masquerading humbugs. The utter and well-
founded contempt in which the experienced news-
paper-man holds 99 per cent, of politicians is too
deep to be expressed in words.
There are exceptions both as to the men and as
to the Occasions, but, these be the general rules.
Instead of being “all beer and skittles” a re-
porter’s life is one “demnition grind,” until after
years of badly-paid toil, should he be lucky and
live, he secures a prize sinecure, and makes others
drag round the grindstone ; or, until he reaches
that commonplace, uninteresting yet placid haven
of shattered ambitious, a country editorship, and
slowly saturates himself to the last and stillest
haven of all, in muddled complacency, varied with
interludes of vermilion fantods. Sometimes, how-
ever, he hasn’t even the cash to get any fantods.
Only the other day a man convicted of forging an
order for third-rate board and lodging, and sent to
gaol for six months, deposed that he had been
editing a N.S.W. country journal for 10s. a week
and his keep—for less than the stipend of a potato-
peeler in a sailor’s eating-house.
The prizes of a journalistic career are for
the few—for the mediocre man, generally, who
is steady and saving and servile; not for the
brilliant but erratic genius, who is generally a
generous and often a reckless spendthrift—a
philanthropist to all save himself, and, if he be
married, his wife and family. Let me apprentice
CrammUm to the noble craft of what is called, but
really te not, “journalism.”
* * #
He who aims at becoming a good, all-round
newspaper-man should commence at the “case”
the typesetter’s “frame.” Intelligent eofii-
positors who afterwards deVelope the literary
instinct are the moat Valuable Wen <t newspaper—
especially a country newspaper—knows. The
apprenticeship means, at least, five years of
drudgery and stewing in a murky room, amid
yellow, smoky, gas-jets, or dazzled by the garish,
steel-blue, eye-paralysing glitter of the electric
light five years (most probably) of night-work—
five years of no-holidays—and, if the compositor
has a soul above his “ frame,” five years of study,
though an intelligent experience of a newspaper
printing-room is an education in itself. These
five years will, however, supply all the technical
knowledge that is requisite afterwards to escape
the lurid cursings of the “comps,” and to avoid the
.expense involved by ignorance of technicalities
and consequent blundering,
Speaking of .education, overmuch education Is
not required in a newspaper-man. Your univer-
sity dux is almost always a duffer on a newspaper.
Learn the native tongue—in our oaso, English,
Latin and Greek and dead languages generally
are only of the smallest service. Newspaper-
readers don’t want them, can’t understand them
for the most part, and, when they can understand
them, despise the writer as a pedantic showman,
French and German are far more useful, but only
that you may exchange ideas with newspapers
printed in those tongues. Learn English—that is
the essential. There are now about 250,000 words in
the English language, of which the ordinary
Briton knows and uses about 10 000. Learn
100,000 in addition, and then go right ahead.
The ordinary reporter has only about twice the
vocabulary of an Australian blackfellow and never
makes any progress in consequence. His two
dull, bald-headed flowers of rhetoric consist 111
saying that a man is “launched into eternity”
when he means that he is hanged, and in alluding
to a fire in a rag-shop as “the devouring element.”
Suppose, however, that Crammum is too high-
toned to defile his fingers with printer’s ink as a
practical compositor. He should, at the very
least, start as an assistant reader, the lowest rung
of the literary ladder. A year at this, if it doesn’t
kill him, should qualify him to become a full-
fledged reader, and give him an inner-circle know-
ledge of the working mechanism of a newspaper.
He can also take an oc asional chance to report
an unimportant occurrence, and so get his hand
in. Then he attacks shorthand and type-writing.
A terribly hard year’s toil, this. A reader’s hours
generally range (on a morning newspaper) from
7 p.m. (sometimes as early as 4 p.-m.) until 3 or 4
or 5 o’clock next morning, with a desperate plunge
at a gobbled sandwicli about midnight. Wages:
Reader, from £2 10s. to £4 par week, according to
the status of the paper; assistant, from £1 to £2.
* * #
I now promote Crammum to a junior-
reportership, and take him on the staff at £2 per
week.
He is the reporting rouseabout. All the driest,
most sickening, most insignificant “engage-
ments’’fall to his lot. His only relief is in the
Police-court; he does see some fun there and, I
am deeply pained to say, ho indulges in small and
sinful romances there as well. When lie reports
that “ Anno Smith, a prepossessing young woman,
neatly attired,” was charged witli larceny, it is
s ife to bet that ho is badly “ gone ” upon Anne
Smith, and takes a deep interest in that tearful
creature. The present writer speaks from experi-
ence, To him that bright though erring maiden,
Anne Smith, was a vision that will never entirely
fade away. Then sometimes Crammum gets hold
of a sensation and works it up with an enthusiastic
frehity. He seldom gets ptaisfetl for it, although
his imaginative elaboration sells hundreds of extra
copies of the paper. His virtue is its own reward.
But he is enthusiastic, he is capable and promis-
ing, and he is a glutton for work. He gets tons of
it, and helps to make a fortune for the man who
pays him a miserable screw. Here is a sample
day Office at 9.30 a.m. to look at the “engage-
ment book” and initial his “engagements,” in
order to show that lie holds himself responsible to
supply a report of each ; 10 o’clock, Police-coni t;
12 o’clock, see coroner’s orderly and abridge, de-
positions of unimportant inquest; 3 o’clock, attend
building society’s meeting (no sensation or
tiothing but a fat man, with a voice like the hum
of a bumble-bee, reading acres of figures); 4 o’clock,
attend ordinary Weekly meeting of hospital
Committee; 7 o’clock, attend Wesleyan tea-
meeting and , subsequent speechifying, and con-
dense three hours’ stale buns and ghastly
drivel into 20 lines of print ; 10 o’clock,
the “rounds.” The “rounds” mean that ho
has to hunt up and report up n anything that
has happened during the day in the nature of
police oases, or casualties at the hospital, or inci-
dents at the fire-brigade station, or the morgue,
including epileptic fits, lost dogs, or anything else
that has an official outlet. In these days of tele-
phonic communication the “ rounds ” are much
lightened. In my junior reporting days I had to
rush round personally to the hospital, the central
police barracks, the detective office, the fire-
brigades, the dead-house, and other places, and
was received everywhere with a cold and arrogant
eye. The only joy to a junior then “doing the
rounds” was that he Would “foot it ’’regularly,
and conscientiously charge ’bus or cab-hire, thus
pocketing “exs.” as a welcome addition to his
meagre scicw.
Crammum has not been a reporter long before
his ideas of the English language are considerably
modified. He has to unlearn the polite and
elaborate style of the schools, and to acquire
“ newspaper English,” with its inflexible idioms.
This branch of the subject, however, requires a
chapter to itself.
Crammum soon gets an idea of the incompressi-
bility of “ space,” and learns to gauge the “ news-
paper value ” of events. The sub-editor often
hands him back two columns of his enthusiastic
brain cobweb, with the curt instruction, petu-
lently growled—“ Boil that down to a-third of a
column.” 'I his perhaps at 11 p.ra., and he hasn’t
had a bite since breakfast.
But his era of glory comes at last. He becomes
a recognised reliable shorthand-writer, and his
descriptive writing “takes.” Now, he slaves in
the Parliamentary gallery, hobnobs with hon.
members, attends deputations, and interviews
Ministers, goes to banquets and receptions, gets
glorious country trips, dress-suits it at the theatres
only to find that these much-envied pursuits
have no charm for him, but are all the same
solemn grind of the same solemn grindstone. The
hardened reporter would make, notes of a dog-
fight or a general resurrection with the same
funereal impassibility. He has lost the capacity
for enjoyment. Besides, the intimacy with the
“ great ones ” is all hollow, and the “Personages ”
really regard him as a flunkey, though they treat
him with (sometimes cold and sometimes cordial)
politeness. Still, he lias mounted a few rungs
higher. Always supposing that by this time, he
has not become a confirmed drunkard, and got the
kick-out from about 17 offices.
The talented drunkard becomes a flaneur, whose
manuscript is accepted at the offices where his
personal presence is voted a nuisance. However,
he is 110 slave at the beck and call of any task-
master ; he follows his own erratic promptings
and gets lots of “fun,” followed by lots of
“horrors.” He is generally buried on the cheap
at the sloppy end of the cemetery, and has no
tombstone, and leaves nothing behind him but
old broken pipes and bad debts.
But Crammnm isn’t that sort. He is steady
and reliable, and becomes leader of the reporting
staff in the course of years, and makes the juniors
grind at the mill as ho had to do, takes all the
creamy “engagements” himself, and calmly puts
in his own name as “among the distinguished
personages present on the occasion.” Nevertheless,
he is personally responsible for the staff’s work, and
ho is always being ear-pulkd by the irate pro-
prietor, or editor, or managing-director, with the
“sack ” grimly looming in the distance.
Perhaps Crammum slips gently on to a Han-
SABD-stafl —which is certainly a billet for life, but
nothing more. There, he simply vegetates and dis-
appears from public glory, and the balance of his
existence is spent copying the dull, flabby speeches
of the man with the iron jaw and the fat-headed
member who wants the Bible read in schools. Or,
he becomes sub-editor, bullied by the editor and
himself bullying the reporters. Crammnm now
finds that he has longer hours in his hot and
stuffy room than he had when he was reader. His
“screw ” is better, but his responsibilities are ten
times heavier than his “screw”—which varies,
according to the status of the “rag,’’from £5 to
£9 per week. He is responsible for everything—
errors in the editorials, any “ slummoeky ” work
on the part of the reporters ; errors in cablegrams,
wrong spelling, wrong punctuation, a mistake, in
some old Sanscrit quotation, a wrong date on some
event that happened 1200 years ago, or a blunder
about the policy of the leader of the Opposition in
Bulgaria—all these mishaps are passed on to the
devoted sub. He has to know more than Solomon
and Moses and the seven wise men of Greece, and
never to be tired.
Perhaps Crammnm becomes at last “ The
Editor.” More likely he doesn’t. If he does, it
is his turn to do as little as he can for as big a
salary as he can get. He pays his “leader-
writers” per article, and they are only too
glad to take all they can earn. He has, of
course, to pen one or two editorials a week
himself, and generally to “put in his time.”
But only one reporter out of 50 reaches this
height of “Wc-dom” (copyright). The odds
against Crammnm are too many.
If Crammun proves himself only a mediocre
journalist he becomes a country editor. If he has
saved money or gets a windfall, ho buys a country
paper, and becomes a local magnate. But the
hired country editor has a poor, if placid life.
His eloquence sheds lustre on the big turnip, the
double-headed calf, the snake story, and on the
enterprise of his best advertiser the soulless local
storekeeper. He is never beard of outside his own
domain of three selections and a gully, but he
gets free drinks and dies of alcoholic pleurisy and
Bright’s disease.
Such is journalism, Crammum. Are its chances
inviting enough to allure you from the fields of
tuition? Is it worth going for? Had I a son I
would sooner make him a bricklayer than a
journalist. Law, medicine, holy orders, architec-
ture, civil engineering, surveying, accountancy,

THEODORE GRUBSTREET.
The native question was, at latest aclvices>
unsolved in New Guinea. Some trader had
visited the interior with a rifle and an assortment
of glass beads, and the natives slew him and ate
him. Then the whole Government stall left oil
fanning itself, and got up, and sallied forth on a
punitive cum*exploratory expedition and caught
two savages with skewers through _ their noses
somewhere in the “disturbed district. Some
other native having been understood to say that
they were the guilty nig?, they were hauled along
to Port Moresby and put in the local prison, it
was then that unforeseen troubles harassed the
course of British justice. Somebody gave a teed
of cold pork and pickles to one of the prisoners,
and the unusual diet settled him before his case
same on. It struck him about where his waistcoat
ought to have been, and he curled up and died.
He had scarcely reached the golden shore when it
was discovered that the other nig. was suffering
from a peculiarly horrible kind of leprosy, so the
authorities entered a nolle prosequi against him.
They did it by opening the prison door and crying
“ Shoo ! ” through the window until the leper fled
to his native wilds. Then they fumigated the
gaol and adjourned the Criminal Sessions.
Lady Burton was offered 6000 guineas for
the MS. of her deceased husband’s translation of
an Arabic work, entitled “The Scented Garden,
said to have been made for “ scientific and
psychological purposes.” Lady Burton refused
the offer, and, believing that most men would read
the work for “filth’s sake,” declared that she
wouldn’t have it preserved for six millions of
guineas. “Sorrowfully, reverently, and in fear
and trembling,” said she, “I burnt sheet after
sheet, until the whole of the volumes were con-
sumed.” A heated controversy is still proceeding
over the matter. Thus Robert Buchanan :
On the same plea as hers, a Roman Catholic wife might
consider herself justified in destroying a masterpiece
written to expose the evils of Roman Catholicism ; or a
Protestant daughter, left heiress of a Roman Catholic
father’s unwihlished writings, might suppress and burn a
work from' the Romish point of view. In either case,
there would be a sincere desire to save the beloved one
from eternal damnation, to “cut the cords which pie-
vent his poor soul from “ ascending to Heaven. ’ Now,
questions of Morality are far more difficult to determine
than questions of dogmatic Religion, and little or no good
is gained by the Podsnapian method of putting all such
questions aside as “offensive,” “improper, 1 ‘ not to he
discussed by decent people.” , , v
“ No man can save another s Soul,
Or pay another’s debt.”
says the poet; and if Sir Richard Burton left a base book
as his legacy to mankind, no post-mortem action on the
part of his wife could save or absolve him. We who
plead for freedom of individual expression, however, deny
the right of any person to tamper with literature and to
set aside the will of a great personality. We decline to
let the voice of homely virtue or superstitious prejudice
penetrate to our death-chambers. Had Sir Richard
Burton said to his wife —“Here is a book—but you must
decide whether or not it is fit for publication,” the matter
would have been very different. He gave her no s ich
choice. In burning the work of his mature and deliberate
creation, and in afterwards announcing to the world that
she had done so because the work was foul, she stultified
her own devotion and she stigmatised its object in the
eyes of all mankind.
The amalgamation of the Exchange Bank
of Australia, Ltd., with the Sydney Deposit Bank,
King-street, is a step in the right direction and as
following in the line of the English banks, a great
number of whom are adopting this satisfactory
method of concentrating their energies and
capital. The total business of the Exchange
Bank has been merged- into that of the Sydney
Deposit Bank, and under the able management of
Mr. T. S. Richardson, the General Manager, con-
tinued success is assured. The latest development
in banking has been promulgated by the Sydney
Deposit Bank who have for some time past given
practical effect to the principle of equitably
dividing banker’s profits with depositors’. The
Exchange Department is very interesting, and
travellers to all parts of the world are informed
that they can obtain or dispose of coins and notes
of all nations, also circular notes of exchange are
issued negotiable in all the principal cities through-
out the world.*

GOLDEN SHANTY.
The History of a Jackeroo.
IN FIVE LETTERS.
NO. I.
Letter from Joscelyn de Greene, of Wiltshire, Eng-
land, to College friend.
Deah Gus, —The Governor has fixed things up for
me at last. lam not to go to India, but to Aus-
tralia. It seems the Governor met some old Aus-
tralian swell named Moneygrub at a dinner in the
City. He has thousands of acres of land and
herds of sheep, and I am to go out and learn the
business of sheep-raising. Of course it is not
quite the same as going to India; but some really
decent people do go out to Australia sometimes, I
am told, and I expect it won’t be so bad. In
India one generally goes into the Civil Service,
nothing to do and lots of niggers to wait on you,
but the Australian Civil Service no fellow can
well go into—it is awful low business, I hear. _ I
have been going in for gun and revolver practice
so as to be able to hold my own against the
savages and the serpents in the woods of Aus-
tralia. Mr. Moneygrub says there isn’t much
fighting with the savages nowadays; but, he says,
the Union shearers will give me all the fight I
want. What is a Union shearer, I wonder ? My
mother has ordered an extra-large artist’s umbrella
for me to take with me for fear of sunstroke, and
I can hold it over me while watching the flocks.
She didn’t half like my going until Mr. Moneygrub
said that they always dressed for dinner at the
head station, and that a Church of England
clergyman visits there twice a month. I am
only to pay a premium of £5OO for the experience,
and Mr. Moneygrub says I’ll be able to make
that out of scalps in my spare time. He says
there is a Government reward for scalps. I don’t
mind a brush with the savages, but if he thinks
I’m going to scalp my enemies he is mistaken.
Anyhow, I sail next week, so no move from yours,
outward bound, Joscelyn de Greene.
# * *
No. 11.
Letter from Moneygrub and Co., London, to the
Manager of the Company's “ Dryhone ”
Station, Paroo River, Australia.
Dear Sir, —We beg to advise you of having
made arrangements to take a young gentleman
named Greene as colonial-experiencer, and he will
be consigned to you by the next boat. His
premium is £5OO, and you will please deal with
him in the usual way. Let us know when you
have vacancies for any more colonial-experiencers,
as several are now asking about it, and the
premiums are forthcoming. You _ are on no
account to employ Union shearers this year ; and
you must cut expenses as low as you can. Would
it not be feasible to work the station with the
colonial-experience men and Chinese labour? &c.,
&c., &c.
* # *
No. 111.
Letter from Mr. Robert Saltbush, of Fryingpan
Station, to a friend.
Dear Billy, —Those fellows over at Drybqne
Station have been at it again. You know it joins
us, and old Moneygrub, who lives in London,
sends out an English bloke every now and again
to be a jackeroo. He gets £5OO premium for each
one, and the manager puts the jackeroo to boun-
dary-ride a tremendous great paddock at the back
of the run, and he gives him a week’s rations
and tells him never to go through a gate, because
so long as he only gets lost in the paddock lie can
always be found somehow, but if he gets out of
the paddock, Lord knows whether he’d ever be
seen again. And there these poor English devils
are, riding round the fences and getting lost and
not seeing a soul until they go near mad from
loneliness; and then they run away at last, and
old Macgregor, the manager, he makes a great fuss
and goes after them with a whip, but he takes
care to have a stockman pick their tracks up and
take them to the nearest township, and then they
go on the spree and never come back, and old
Moneygrub collars the £5OO and sends out
another jackeroo. It’s a great game. The
last one they had was a fellow called Greene.
They had him at the head station for a while,
letting him get pitched off the station horses. He
said: “They’re awfully beastly horses in this
country, by Jove; they’re not content with throw-
ing you off, but they’d kick you afterwards if you
don’t be careful.” When they got full up of him
at the head station they sent him out to the big
paddock to an old hut full of fleas, and left him
there with his tucker and two old screws of horses.
The horses, of course, gave him the slip, and he
got lost for two days looking for them, and his
meat was gone bad when he got home. He killed
a sheep for tucker, and how do you think he killed
it? He shot it! It was a ram, too, one of Money-
grub’s best rams, and there will be the deuce to
pay when they find out. About the fourth day a
swagman turned up, and he gave the swaggy a
gold watch-chain to show him the way to the
nearest town, and he is there now—on the spree, I
believe. He had a fine throat for whisky, any-
how, and the hot climate has started him in
earnest. Before he left the hut and the fleas, he
got a piece of raddle and wrote on the door :
HELL.
S.R.O.
whatever that means. I think it must be some
sort of joke. The brown colt I got from Ginger is
a clinker, a terror to kick, but real fast. He takes
a lot of rubbing out for half a mile, &c., &c., &o.
# # *
No. IV.
Letter from Sandy M‘Gregor, manager of Dryhone
Station, to Messrs. Moneygrub & Co., London,
Dear Sirs, —I regret to have to inform you
that the young gentleman, Mr. Greene, whom you
sent out, has seen fit to leave his employment and
go away ti the township. No doubt he found
the work somewhat rougher than he had been
used to, but if young gentlemen are sent out here
to get experience they must expect to rough it
like other bushmen. I hope you will notify his
friends of the fact: and if you have applications
for any more colonial experiences we now have a
vacancy for one. There is great trouble this year
over the shearing, and a lot of grass will be burnt
unless some settlement is arrived at, &c., &c., &c.
* * *
Extract from evidence of Senior-constable Rafferty,
taken at an inquest before Lushington, P.M.
for the North-east by South Paroo distinct, and
a jury.
I am a senior-constable, stationed at Walloopna
beyant. On the 6th instant, I received informa-
tion that a man was in the horrors at Flanagan’s
hotel. I went down and saw the man, whom I
recognise as the deceased. He was in the horrors:
lie was very bad. He had taken all his clothes
off, and was hiding in a fowl-house to get away
from the devils which were after him. I went to
arrest him, but he avoided me, and escaped over
a paling-fence on to the Queensland side of the
border, where I had no power to ax-rest him. He
was foaming at the mouth and acting like a mad-
man. He had been on the spree for several days.
From enquiries made, I believe his name "o be
Greene, and that he had lately left the emiUy-
rnent of Mi-. Macgregor, at lirybone. He was
found dead on the roadside by the carriers coming
into Walloopna. Ho had evidently wandered
away from the township, and died from the effects
of the sun and the drink.
Verdict of jury : “That deceased came to his
death by sunstroke and exposure during a fit of
delirium tremens, caused by excessive drinking.
No blame attached to anybody.” Curator of
intestate estates advertises for next of kin of
J. Greene, and nobody comes forward. Curtain !
THE BANJO.
During the winter season many persons of
means and who care not for the cold, migrate to
warmer climes, and the North of Queensland is
visited by numbers of colonists who cannot stand
the keener blasts of our own colony. There are
great numbers, however, who are not able to meet
the expense of such a trip, or who cannot leave
Sydney for business reasons. To these the advan-
tages of a sanatorium, in which they could, with-
out leaving the metropolis, enjoy all the benefits of
sea-bathing coupled with the comforts of a first-
class hotel, would be a boon indeed. Yet such a
combination is to be found at their very doors,
so to speak, and persons who would travel
hundreds of miles to be able to obtain a regular
course of sea-bathing at this period of the
year—if it were possible without the unpleasant-
ness of shivering in the cold ocean blasts—are
very frequently in ignorance of the fact. At the
Natatorium, in Pitt-strect, however, near to the
Haymarket, rheumatic subjects or invalids may
have their diurnal dip in sc a-water of a tempera-
ture of 95deg., newly-pumped from Port Jackson
throughout the day and heated by means of
steam. In the hotel connected with the baths,
which is one of the finest in the city, every
accommodation is provided for visitors, and in
addition to a first-class menu, there are all the
comforts of a home. Many persons already take
advantage of this fact, and the joint attractions
of the baths and the hostelry draw a constant
flow of new visitors to the establishment, which
is now under one control. The Sydney Bathing
Company are now reaping the well-earned reward
of their enterprise and good business-management
in this increase of business.
NOBODY WANTS THAT GOLD RING.
For nearly 100 years a certain family of working
people living in Paris have ended their lives by
suicide. From father to son, from mother to
daughter, has descended a plain gold ring, and on
the finger of every one of these suicides, as they
lay in death, this ring has been found. Only last
year the body of a young man who had killed
himself was brought to the Morgue, and on his
finger was the fatal golden circlet. He was the
last of his race. The ring was buried with the
corpse, from which no one acquainted with its
history will have the courage to remove it.
The mental taint in this family came from some
remote ancestor, and was intensified by their
recognition of it until it became a controlling
force ; and the ring was accepted as imposing upon
its possessor the obligation to commit suicide,
after the example of the person who last wore it.
This form of mania usually originates in a disorder
of the nervous system, which in its turn arises
from anmmia, or poverty of the blood, one of the
results of imperfect nutrition.
A recent letter from a gentleman living in Nor-
folk contains the following assertion: “ I longed
for death ; I was afraid of the night; I was afraid
to he alone, yet I hated society. I teas afraid that
in some one of these hours oj deep gloom and depres-
sion I should lift my hand against my own life, for
I knew that many had done so from the same cause.”
The dark hours became a time of terror to him, so
he says. He tossed and tumbled on his bed,
wondering if morning would ever dawn again. In
this case it was not an accusing conscience, as he
had committed no offence ; the cause was purely
a physical one—yet, all too common in England—
indigestion and dyspepsia, with the long chain of
consequences dragging after it, nervous collapse
among them.
He relates that his skin and eyes had been more
or less discoloured for years, often of a ghastly
and repulsive yellow. This was due to the presence
of bile in the blood and tissues, where it had no
business to be. But as the weak and torpid liver
could not remove it, no other result was possible
than the one our friend experienced. His head
frequently ached as though fiends had turned it
into a workshop, and pains chased one another
through his body as though he had at least half
the maladies catalogued in the popular books of
disease.
Yet one thing, and one only, was responsible
for all the mischief, namely, the poison introduced
into the blood from the decaying food in the
stomach and intestines. The cold feet, the loss
of appetite and ambition, the mental despondency,
the sense of weariness and fatigue, the bad taste
in the mouth, dry cough, giddiness, palpitation,
chills, weakness, &c., are a brood of foul birds
hatched in one nest, and the mother is always
indigestion and dyspepsia.
Time passed somehow, as it always does,
whether we laugh or cry, and this man grew
heartily tired of a life thus burdened and spoiled.
He longed to see the end of it, and no wonder.
But the last page of his letter is pitched in a
higher key. He says, “When I think of what
I was, and what I am now, I can hardly realise
the change. For the past six months I have been
using a preparation known as Mother Seigel's
Curative Syrup, and it has actually revolutionised
my whole system. One of my tenants recom-
mended it to me, and I tried it just to please him.
Now I praise it for myself, and thank the men
who made and advertise it. My troubles are over,
and I feel (at 57) as light, elastic, and gay as a
boy on his summer vacation. I tell my doctors
they are beaten at their own trade by an old
German nurse, and so far as I am concerned they
can’t deny it. I have no more horrible thoughts
of self-destruction, for I find too much enjoyment
in living. My thanks are too deep for words.”
The author of this letter consents to the pub-
lication of so much of it as is here printed, but
declines to allow the use of his name, at least for
the present, for reasons we are bound to respect.
But the evident sincerity of his story will carry
conviction to every candid mind.

The Great Trousers Question.
The Executive Board of the United States
Women’s National Council has issued a decree
that on and after June 1, 1892, business women —
type-writers, sales-girls, cashiers, compositors,
and clerks —shall begin wearing trousers. It is
not clear how the Women’s National Council pro-
poses to enforce its decree. An attempt to force
a young lady into a pair of trousers against her
will would constitute an assault in law and might
be resisted. In prisons and hospitals refractory
inmates are thrust into strait-waistcoats in spite
of their struggles; but the conformation of the
garment which is usually regarded as exclusively
masculine opposes difficulties which could not be
easily overcome in the case of a lady. For this, if
for no other reason, a court would bo slow to issue
a writ of mandate requiring a lady to don the
breeches ; it is against public policy for courts to
issue orders which cannot be executed. The
decree of the council must be regarded as merely
advisory and not mandatory.
It must be assumed that, before taking their
stand on the trousers question, the members of
the council had satisfied themselves* that the bulk
of their constituents were in favour of a change;
that female-workers are eager to borrow from
men their nether garments, as their fashionable
sisters have already borrowed male hats, coats,
waistcoats and shirts. In this light we can see
that the unwritten law, which has abolished a
multiplicity of skirts and substituted a maillot,
may have been a premonition of a further
approach toward uniformity of dress between the
sexes. It may, perhaps, be regarded as
typical of the determination of women to
a sort the absolute equality of the txo halves
of mankind. It is said of a man who is hen-
pecked that his wife wears the bi'eeches. May
this movement be notice to husbands that they
are all hereafter to belong to the hon-pecked class
—without always having the privilege of choosing
the hen ?
Whatever the true inwardness of the impending
revolution may be, it will not encounter much
opposition from men. Trousers are a wide insti-
tution, in which there is room for both sexes. If
the darlings want them, they may have them and
welcome. They have plenty of example for covet-
ing the forked garment. To say nothing of the
disciples of Mrs. Bloomer and Mrs. Swisshelm in
this country, and the shining precedent set by
Dr. Mary Walker, more than one-half the women
of the world are wearing trousers at this moment.
An Asiatic or a Turkish lady, or a lady from
North Africa, would no sooner go out without
her trousers than a gentleman would walk down
Market-street in like dishabille. She may flirt
with her tunic, and her undershirt, and her robe,
and her short skirt and flounces, doff or don them
as her fancy and the weather suggests ; but she
would rather die than take off her trousei’s. They
are the base of her costume. In China she sleeps
in them ; she would probably do so in Turkey,
likewise, but that it is the fashion in that unspeak-
able country to go to bed without any clothes at
all.
Whether trousers are more comfortable than
skirts is a question which cannot bo answered,
because there is no one living who has had suffi-
cient experience of both to decide. It would take
a modern Tiresias to solve the problem. But
unless our conceptions of the lines of beauty are
warped by habit, men will agree that the skirt is
more be oming to a woman than the male gar-
ment. No one ever admires a lady in a Turkish
or a Circassian costume at a fancy ball. She is a
nondescript, and the beholder is impelled to be-
seech tier to dispense with one of her two sets of
apparel. As old Gascoigne said ;—
“ What be they? women? masking in men’s weeds?
With dutchkin doublets, and with jerkins jagged?
With Spanish spangs, and ruffs fet out of France?
With high-capt hats, and feathers (limit a-flaunt?
They be so, sure, even Wo to man indeed.”
Still, if the fashion of trousers once gets a foot-
hold, it will not be checked by considerations of
what appears for the hour to be becoming. The
eye is quickly educated to changes of costume.
Twenty-five years ago, a woman who did not wear
a bell-hoop was laughed at. Everybody remembers
Leech’s inimitable caricatures of Miss La Jeunc,
who would hot wear hoops. Now a lady in a
wide-fitting hoop would be followed by the boys
in the street. A hundred and eighty years ago
Addison described a fashionable lady who was
arrested* and ordered to be brought into court.
She declared that she had tried two or three times
to come in, but could not, by reason of the size of
the petticoat she wore, which could not be
squeezed through the door. It was necessax-y to
impaixel a special jury of matrons to remove so
much of her costume as impeded her entrance,
•without shocking her modesty. She was acquitted,
but in endeavouring to return home with the
obnoxious hoop on, she fell, whereupon the essayist
declared that her prostrate form presented the
appearance of a bell without a clapper. Eighty
years after that, ladies of fashion wore skirts so
limp and scanty that they impeded the action in
walking. Mine. Recamier’s skirts wx-apped
tightly round her beautiful limbs. Taste changes,
like public opinion, and no one can say that in the
year 1909 a well-dressed girl may not be one in
well-fitting trousers, tight at the knee and clinging
closely round the body from the waist down.
An actress in the costume of a page always
looks mis-shapen. But that is because our eye is
accustomed to see forked raiiyent on a masculine
figure, moulded to masculine proportions.
Sculptors generally consider the female form
more artistically perfect than the male. The
curves arc more harmonious and the general effect
more pleasing. If that is so, it is prejudice
begotten of custom which offends the eye when a
well-proportioned woman appears in public in
male attire, and, after people’s taste had been
educated to the change, they would rebel against
a reactionary movement that threatened to hide
so much loveliness from inference.
When Mrs. Bloomer devised the costume which
bears her name, which consisted of a pair of
trousers and a Very short skirt, it was satirized all
over the world, and a good many women who were
disposed to relieve the sex of the dragging weight
of many skirts were bullied into rejecting it. But
most of the criticisms were the work of men to
whom female underclothing must always be a
mystery. The blow which killed the reform in
England was a sentence in the London Times,
written by a male ignoramus. He said: “The
bloomer dress is very pretty, no doubt; but we
should like to see a woman who wore it pick up a
pin.”— Aikjonaut.
Broken Hill Proprietary mine returns
£96,000 monthly with the greatest regularity.
A well-known rock in Gray Harbour,
Straits of Magellan, is now used as a fine, bold,
picturesque medium for advertising Pears’ soap.
London Daily News relates that when the
s.s. Chimborazo, during a recent excursion to
Norway, struck a rock, the captain promptly
ordered out the boats, which were soon filled in
the usual order of “ladies first.” “I will never
leave without my husband,” protested one lady as
the third boat was lowered. “ Make yourself
easy, my dear madam,” was the answer, “ he left
by the first boat.”
A voice cried from the grave at Port
Arthur, Tas., the other day. Some workmen who
were pulling down the seats in the Modern Prison
Chapel, found behind the panelling a prisoner’s
letter, written apparently to the Commandant.
Here are some extracts :
I ask you it you were to gasp your last to night, could
you, in your last moments, die with any composure with
the thoughts of not only dozens, hut perhaps scores of
unfortunates who have sworn to revenge, to murder both
you and your family, with that little hoy who calls you
papa; that the innocent must suffer for your bloody
tyranny? If you had any respect for your pom - children
when you are gone, surely you would act with justice.
Does the little present repay you for all the evil which
will come on you and yours sooner or later? You ought
to be ashamed of yourself; you are none the better
for your tyranny and severity. Why not change, and
let not so many of your fellow creatures seek
anil cry for your blood and the blood of your
children? Many are the changes in life. You
may yet he reduced lo poverty, if it is true what the
Bible says, “ lie that thinketh he standeth take heed lest
he fall,” and “ Whatsoever you sow that shall you reap.”
For my part I believe there is neither God, devil, heaven,
nor hell. . . . But this is nothing (o the question’
you are a tyrant . . . Your dear little hoy who calls
you papa, the innocent of your heart, you are bringing
him up to pay for your tyranny and oppression, but
. . . it will come home to you and yours sooner or
later. My four years will soon wear away, and then you
may look out for squalls.
The letter is long, and becomes more and more
vigorously abusive of all the officials, charging
them with peculation and other misconduct. The
genuineness of this letter is vouched for by Rev.
J. B. Woolnough, now living at the then Hell-
upon-Earth. And the writer ! what of him ?
Did he ever “do” his remaining four years, or,
driven by a maddening system to some rash deed,
die at the hands of the tyranny ?
“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.

Religious Interference i.i Politics.

r i he Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
ar.d has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once bo seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin's inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success ir
Australian journalism, the one completely non
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has over seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now alflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kuoblmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted,
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, roasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectoratingevery day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
•nd of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEEN AN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smythe, J.P,
